Complete vs Deficient Performance in Hanafi Usul
Definition and illustration of two types of performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and deficient performance in Hanafi usul and apply the ruling for each type to concrete fiqh scenarios.

Core Idea
Complete performance discharges liability immediately; deficient performance only exits liability if the item is destroyed by an act of God.

This lesson defines two fundamental categories of fulfilling obligations: complete performance (adāʾ kāmil) and deficient performance (adāʾ qāṣir). Through concrete examples from prayer, circumambulation, sales, and usurpation, the author shows how the ruling on discharge depends on the quality of performance. The dispute over taʿdīl al-arkān (proper execution of pillars) reveals how the same act can be classified differently across Hanafi sub-schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types and their definitions
Locate the opening statement that divides performance into two types. Focus on the key phrase 'تسليم عين الواجب' (delivering the substance of the obligation) and note the conditions of 'كمال صفته' versus 'نقصان في صفته'.
The definition hinges on whether the attribute (ṣifa) is perfect or deficient.
2
Examine the examples for complete performance
Look at each example: prayer with taʿdīl al-arkān, circumambulation with wuḍūʾ, handing over a sold item sound, returning a usurped item as it was. Identify the common feature: the substance is delivered with all required qualities intact.
These examples illustrate complete performance across different chapters of fiqh.
3
Understand the legal effect of complete performance
The text states 'يحكم بالخروج عن العهدة به' – the obligor is judged to have exited liability. Note that this holds even if the recipient is unaware (e.g., feeding the owner his own food).
Complete performance instantly and unconditionally discharges liability.
4
Analyze the examples for deficient performance
Review examples: prayer without taʿdīl al-arkān, circumambulation without wuḍūʾ, returned item encumbered with debt or criminal liability, paying defective coins. Identify the missing quality in each.
Deficiency can be in the act itself (prayer) or in the object's condition (encumbered item).
5
Determine the legal effect of deficient performance
The text rules that if the item perishes by a heavenly calamity, the obligor is discharged. Understand the reasoning: the substance was delivered, and the deficiency cannot be remedied after destruction.
Deficient performance does not immediately discharge; it becomes effective only upon destruction by act of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الواجب بحكم الأمر نوعان: أداء كامل وأداء قاصر. فالأداء الكامل: تسليم عين الواجب مع كمال صفته، نحو الصلاة بتعديل الأركان والطواف متوضئاً وتسليم المبيع سليماً كما اقتضاه العقد، ورد المغصوب كما كان، وحكم هذا النوع أن يحكم بالخروج عن العهدة به. وعلى هذا قلنا: الغاصب إذا باع المغصوب أو وهبه وسلمه إلى المالك يخرج عن العهدة ويكون ذلك أداء حقه ويلغو ما صرح به من البيع والهبة. ولو غصب طعاماً فأطعمه مالكه وهو لا يدري أنه طعامه، أو غصب ثوباً فألبسه مالكه وهو لا يدري أنه ثوبه يكون ذلك أداء لحقه. والمشتري في البيع الفاسد لو أعار المبيع من البائع أو رهنه عنده أو آجره منه أو باعه البائع أو وهبه له وسلمه يكون ذلك أداء لحقه ويلغو ما صرح به من الإعارة والرهن والإجارة والبيع والهبة. وأما الأداء القاصر فهو تسليم عين الواجب مع النقصان في صفته نحو الصلاة بدون تعديل الأركان أو الطواف محدثاً ورد المبيع مشغولاً بالدين أو بالجناية ورد المغصوب مشغولاً بالدين أو الجناية أو مباح الدم بالقتل أو أداء الزيوف مكان الجياد إذا لم يعلم الدائن ذلك. وحكم هذا النوع أنه إن هلك المغصوب والمبيع في يد المالك والمشتري بآفة سماوية برئت ذمة الغاصب والبائع.
Chapter: The obligatory by command is of two types: complete performance and deficient performance. Complete performance: delivering the very thing obligated with the perfection of its attribute, such as prayer with proper execution of pillars, circumambulation in a state of ablution, handing over the sold item in sound condition as required by the contract, returning the usurped item as it was. The ruling of this type is that one is judged to have been discharged of liability. Based on this we said: if the usurper sells the usurped item or gifts it and hands it over to the owner, he is discharged and that constitutes fulfillment of his duty, and his declared sale or gift is cancelled. If he usurps food and feeds it to its owner without the owner knowing it is his food, or usurps a garment and clothes its owner without the owner knowing it is his garment, that is fulfillment of his duty. In a void sale, if the buyer lends the sold item to the seller, or pawns it with him, or rents it to him, or the seller sells it or gifts it to him and hands it over, that is fulfillment of his duty, and the declared loan, pawn, rent, sale, gift are cancelled. As for deficient performance, it is delivering the very thing obligated with a deficiency in its attribute, such as prayer without proper execution of pillars, circumambulation without ablution, returning the sold item encumbered with debt or criminal liability, returning the usurped item encumbered with debt or criminal liability, or killed in retaliation, or paying defective coins in place of good ones when the creditor is unaware of that. The ruling of this type is that if the usurped item or sold item perishes in the hands of the owner or buyer by a heavenly calamity, the liability of the usurper or seller is discharged.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of two types of performance (ada) in legal obligations. The author distinguishes complete performance (ada kamil) from deficient performance (ada qasir). Complete performance occurs when the very thing owed is delivered with all required qualities intact. Examples include prayer with proper uprightness of postures (ta'dil al-arkan), circumambulation while in wudu, handing over a sold item in perfect condition, and returning a usurped item as it was at the time of usurpation. The legal effect of complete performance is that the obligor is fully discharged. The author then applies this rule to cases: if a usurper sells or gifts the usurped item to the original owner, the transaction is ignored and the return itself counts as discharge. Similarly, if the usurper feeds the owner's food to him without the owner's knowledge, that counts as return because the owner received the substance. In a voidable sale (bay' fasid), if the buyer lets the seller use the item through a loan or pawn or rent, or even if the seller re-purchases it or receives it as a gift, the physical handover discharges the buyer's duty to return. Deficient performance involves delivering the owed item but lacking a required quality. Examples: prayer without ta'dil al-arkan (disputed between Hanafi scholars), circumambulation without wudu, returning a sold item that has become subject to a debt or criminal liability, returning a usurped item with such defects, or paying counterfeit coins. The ruling: if the item perishes by an act of God after such deficient delivery, the obligor is still discharged because the substance was handed over, though the quality was lacking. This illustrates the principle that performance (ada) focuses on delivery of the 'ayn (specific item), not necessarily perfection, but deficiency affects the ruling of discharge if the item is destroyed.

Key Terms
أداء كامل (adāʾ kāmil): تسليم عين الواجب مع كمال صفته
أداء قاصر (adāʾ qāṣir): تسليم عين الواجب مع النقصان في صفته
تعديل الأركان (taʿdīl al-arkān): الاطمئنان في الركوع والسجدتين والقومة والجلسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a person returns a usurped item that is now encumbered with a debt (e.g., the usurper took a loan on it), and then the item is destroyed by a flood in the hands of the owner, what is the ruling?
A) The usurper remains liable because the return was deficient.
B) The usurper is discharged because the very item was returned, even if deficient.
C) The usurper is discharged only if the owner accepted the item knowingly.
D) The usurper remains liable because the debt was not cleared.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between complete and deficient performance is crucial for determining when an obligor is discharged; the delivery of the substance itself can suffice even if lacking perfection, under specific conditions.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Types of Wajib by Command: Perfect and Deficient Performance
Defining two types of wajib based on performance: perfect and deficient

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define deficient performance (ada' qasir) and apply the Hanafi compensation rule (jabr bi al-mithl) to examples from prayer, tawaf, sales, and usurpation.

Core Idea
Deficient performance fulfills the wajib but may require compensation or incur sin.

In this lesson from Usul al-Shashi, we explore a fundamental Hanafi distinction: the same wajib can be performed either perfectly or with a deficiency in its attribute. The key is whether that deficiency can be compensated by a 'like' — either rationally or legally. This concept governs rulings across worship and transactions.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of ada' qasir
Identify the key components: the same wajib (ayn al-wajib) is delivered, but with a deficiency in its attribute (naqs fi sifatih). This is contrasted with perfect performance.
The deficiency is in the quality, not the substance.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios of compensation
The passage states: if compensation by a like (mithl) is possible, it is required; if not, the deficiency is legally ignored except for sin. Understand what 'mithl' means: a similar act or substitute recognized by reason or law.
Compensation by mithl is a form of jabr.
3
Apply to the given examples
Work through each example: prayer without tranquility, tawaf with impurity, returning a sold item with encumbrances, etc. For each, determine whether compensation by a like is possible and the resulting ruling.
The passage concludes that for tranquility in prayer, no like exists; for the other examples, compensation may be possible.
4
Derive the legal effect
Synthesize: The performance is valid (fulfills the wajib) but deficient. If compensation is possible, it must be done; otherwise the deficiency causes sin but does not invalidate the act.
This classification prevents unnecessary invalidation of acts that are essentially sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الواجب بحكم الأمر نوعان
تسليم عين الواجب مع النقصان في صفته نحو الصلاة بدون تعديل الأركان أو الطواف محدثا و رد المبيع مشغولا بالدين أو بالجناية وردّ المغصوب مباح الدم بالقتل أو مشغولا بالدين أو الجناية بسبب عند الغاصب. وأداء الزيوف مكان الجياد إذا لم يعلم الدائن ذلك، وحكم هذا النوع أنه إن أمكن جبر النقصان بالمثل ينجبر به وإلا يسقط حكم النقصان إلا في الإثم، فهو أداء قاصر. وإن لم يكن جبره بالمثل يسقط حكم النقصان إلا في الإثم. وعلى هذا إذا ترك تعديل الأركان في باب الصلاة لا يمكن تداركه بالمثل عقلا ولا شرعا. إذ لا مثل له عند العبد فسقط، ولو ترك الصلاة في أيام التشريق فقضاها في غير أيام التشريق لا يكبّر؛ لأنه ليس له التكبير بالجهر شرعا، وقلنا في التكبير في أيام التشريق إنما هو المأثور عن خليل الله الجهر فيها وقد مضت فسقط.
Chapter: The wajib by command is of two types: Handing over the same wajib with deficiency in its attribute, such as prayer without proper posture of the pillars, or circumambulation while in a state of impurity, or returning the sold item while it is occupied with debt or a crime, or returning the usurped item which is lawful to kill by killing, or occupied with debt or a crime due to the usurper. And paying bad coins in place of good ones if the creditor does not know that. The ruling of this type is that if compensation for the deficiency is possible with a like, it is compensated thereby; otherwise the ruling of the deficiency is dropped except for sin, so it is deficient performance. And if its compensation is not possible with a like, the ruling of the deficiency is dropped except for sin. Based on this, if one leaves proper posture in the chapter of prayer, it cannot be made up with a like rationally or legally. For there is no like for it with the servant, so it is dropped. And if one leaves prayer during the days of Tashriq and makes it up during other days, he does not recite takbir; because raising the voice in takbir is not legislated for him, and we said that the takbir during the days of Tashriq is the transmitted raising of the voice from the Friend of Allah, and it has passed, so it is dropped.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines the two types of wajib according to command, focusing on the concept of 'ada' qasir' (deficient performance). The author begins by stating the general rule: wajib is of two types, and then describes the first type: handing over the very thing obligated but with a deficiency in its attribute. Examples include praying without proper posture (tadil al-arkan — the pillars of prayer such as bowing and prostrating with tranquility), performing tawaf while in a state of minor impurity (hadath), returning a sold item that has become encumbered with a debt or a crime (like if a slave committed a crime), or returning a usurped item that has become liable for blood money or debt due to the usurper's action. Another example is paying defective coins (zu-yuf) in place of good ones (jiyad) when the creditor is unaware. The ruling for this type is: if the deficiency can be compensated by a similar thing (mithl), it is compensated; otherwise the legal effect of the deficiency is dropped, but the person remains sinful. This is called 'ada' qasir'. The second type is where compensation by a similar thing is impossible, so the deficiency is ignored except for sin. The author then applies this to the example of tadil al-arkan: leaving it out cannot be compensated by a similar act because there is no like for it in prayer, so only sin remains. Similarly, if one misses the takbir during the days of Tashriq (11th-13th of Dhul Hijjah) and makes up the prayer later, he does not say the takbir because raising the voice in takbir is specific to those days and has passed. The commentary by [العلامة] explains that compensation by a like can be either rationally conceivable or legally established; if neither exists, the deficiency is not compensable. The Hanafi position is that tadil al-arkan is wajib (obligatory) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf considers it fard. However, if omitted, the prayer is valid but deficient, and no compensation exists.

Key Terms
أداء قاصر (ada' qasir): Performance in which the same wajib is delivered but with a deficiency in its attribute, such that it is not fully perfect; it may or may not be compensable.
تعديل الأركان (tadil al-arkan): The act of performing the pillars of prayer (ruk'u, sujud, etc.) with tranquility and proper posture.
المثل (al-mithl): A similar thing that can compensate for the deficiency, either rationally conceivable (like a similar act) or legally prescribed (like sajdah al-sahw).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person prays without tranquility in the pillars (tadil al-arkan) according to the passage?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated
B) The prayer is valid but deficient; nothing is due except sin because no compensation is possible
C) The prayer is valid and complete; no deficiency occurred
D) The prayer must be compensated by prostrations of forgetfulness

Key Takeaway
Deficient performance (ada' qasir) fulfills the wajib but may be compensable by a like; if not, only sin remains.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
6 min
Classification of Prohibition: Physical vs. Legal Acts
Defines nahy and classifies it into prohibition of physical and legal acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given prohibition as either physical or legal and explain the distinction based on existence versus ruling.

Core Idea
Prohibition of physical acts is known by reason; prohibition of legal acts depends on revelation for their very existence.

In the chapter on prohibition, Usul al-Shashi introduces a fundamental classification that determines how we understand prohibited acts. This lesson unpacks the distinction between physical acts, whose evil is known independently of revelation, and legal acts, whose very existence as valid acts depends on divine law. Mastering this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define nahy and its default effect
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions of nahy from the text. Note that the chosen view (mukhtar) is that its effect is tahrim (prohibition), a key Hanafi position.
This sets the foundation for understanding prohibition.
2
Distinguish the two types of acts
From the examples given (zina, shurb al-khamr vs. sawm al-Eid, bay' waqt al-nida'), differentiate physical acts (hissi) from legal acts (shar'i). Physical acts are known by reason; legal acts are defined by shara'.
3
Analyze the objection
Consider the objection that both types depend on revelation for their ruling. Understand why this objection might seem plausible.
The objection is based on a conflation of existence and ruling.
4
Apply the distinction by existence
Apply the Hanafi response: the difference lies in existence (wujud). Physical acts exist even without shara' (e.g., zina), while legal acts (e.g., valid salat) have no reality without shara' because they were mujmal (ambiguous).
This is the crucial analytical move.
5
Derive implications for usul
Conclude that the classification is valid and important: physical acts are prohibited due to inherent evil; legal acts are prohibited due to their legal form being violated.
This shapes how we interpret prohibition texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النهي النهي نوعان: نهي عن الأفعال الحسية كالزنا وشرب الخمر والكذب والظلم ونهي عن التصرفات الشرعية كالنهي عن الصوم في العيد والبيع وقت النداء والعبادات المنهي عنها في الأوقات المكروهة. (۱) قوله: [في النهي وهو المنع لغة، وفي اصطلاح أهل الأصول هو استدعاء ترك الفعل بالقول ممن هو دونه، وقيل: هو قول القائل لغيره لا تفعل على سبيل الاستعلاء، واختلفوا في موجبه على اختلافهم في موجب الأمر لاستعماله في معان كثيرة كالتحريم والكراهة وبيان العاقبة والدعاء والشفقة والإرشاد والتحقير واليأس، لكن صيغته مجاز في غير التحريم والكراهة بالاتفاق، والمختار أن موجبه التحريم، كذا في "الفصول". (۲) قوله: [الأفعال الحسّية] وهي التي تعرف حسناً ولا يتوقف تحققها على الشرع كالزنا وشرب الخمر، فإنهما كانا معلومين ماهيةً قبل ورود الشرع وباقية على حالهما ولا يتغيرا بالشرع. (۳) قوله: [كالزنا وشرب الخمر ... إلخ] فإنّها أفعال حسّية يعني: يتحقق حسنها عند من يعلم الشرع ومن لا يعلم، ولا يتوقف وجودها على الشرع، ولقائل أن يقول: لا فرق بين الأفعال الحسية والشرعية؛ لأنك إذا اعتبرت الفرق بينهما باعتبار الوجود فلا شك أنه كما لا يتوقف وجود الزنا وشرب الخمر على وجود الشرع، بل يمكن وجودهما قبل وجود الشرع، فكذا لا يتوقف وجود الصوم والبيع على ورود الشرع وإن اعتبرت الفرق بينهما باعتبار الحكم فلا شك أنه كما يتوقف حكم البيع وهو إيجاب الملك على ورود الشرع، وكذا تتوقف معرفة حكم الزنا وشرب الخمر وهو الحرمة ووجوب الحد على ورود الشرع أيضاً، فلا يستقيم تقسيم النهي إلى النهي عن الأفعال الحسية وإلى النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية أجيب بأنّ الفرق بينهما باعتبار الوجود، فإنّ الأفعال الحسية لا يتوقف وجودها على الشرع وإن توقف حكمها عليه، بخلاف الأفعال الشرعية حيث يتوقف وجودها على الشرع؛ لأنها كانت مجملةً عرفت ببيان الشرع ولا يمكن وجود المجمل إلا ببيان الشرعي فافهم كذا في "المعدن". (٤) قوله: [كالنهي عن الصوم] فإنّ «الصوم» لغةً الإمساك، ثُمّ زيد عليه الوقت والنية والطهارة من الحيض والنفاس شرعاً، و«الصلاة» لغةً الدعاء، وزيد عليها في الشرع أشياء هي الأركان كالقيام والقراءة والركوع والسجود وشروط كالطهارة عن الحدث والخبث وستر العورة والاستقبال والنية، وكذا زيد في البيع والإجارة على المعنى اللغوي أشياء شرعيّة يرجع بعضها إلى الأهل وبعضها إلى المحل، فكانت هذه الأشياء أموراً شرعيةً؛ لأنها توقفت على الشرع، كذا في "المعدن".
Chapter on prohibition: Prohibition is of two types: prohibition of physical acts like adultery, drinking alcohol, lying, and oppression; and prohibition of legal transactions like the prohibition of fasting on the day of Eid, selling during the call to prayer, and acts of worship prohibited at disliked times. (1) His statement: [On prohibition: it is linguistic prevention, and in the terminology of the people of usul, it is a request to abandon an act by speech from one who is superior, and it is said: it is the statement of a person to another: 'Do not do [it]' in a superior manner. They differed regarding its effect just as they differed regarding the effect of command, due to its usage in many meanings like prohibition, dislike, indicating consequence, supplication, compassion, guidance, belittlement, and despair. However, its form is metaphorical in other than prohibition and dislike by consensus, and the chosen view is that its effect is prohibition, as in al-Fusul. (2) His statement: [Physical acts] are those whose goodness is known and whose realization does not depend on the law, like adultery and drinking alcohol. They were known in essence before the arrival of the law and remain as they are, unchanged by the law. (3) His statement: [Like adultery and drinking alcohol... etc.] They are physical acts, meaning their goodness is realized by whoever knows the law and whoever does not, and their existence does not depend on the law. One might say: There is no difference between physical acts and legal acts because if you consider the difference in terms of existence, there is no doubt that just as the existence of adultery and drinking alcohol does not depend on the existence of the law—rather they can exist before the existence of the law—similarly, the existence of fasting and selling does not depend on the arrival of the law. And if you consider the difference in terms of ruling, there is no doubt that just as the ruling of selling—which is the establishment of ownership—depends on the arrival of the law, similarly, knowing the ruling of adultery and drinking alcohol—which is prohibition and the obligation of the hadd penalty—also depends on the arrival of the law. So the division of prohibition into prohibition of physical acts and prohibition of legal acts is not sound. The answer is that the difference between them is in terms of existence, for physical acts do not depend for their existence on the law, even if their ruling depends on it, unlike legal acts, where their existence depends on the law, because they were ambiguous [mujmal] and are known by the law's clarification, and an ambiguous thing cannot exist except by legal clarification. Understand, as in al-Ma'din. (4) His statement: [Like the prohibition of fasting] for 'fasting' linguistically is abstention, then time, intention, and purity from menstruation and postnatal bleeding were added by law. 'Prayer' linguistically is supplication, then things were added by law: pillars like standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration, and conditions like purity from hadath and filth, covering the awrah, facing the qiblah, and intention. Similarly, in selling and leasing, legal things were added to the linguistic meaning, some relating to the capacity and some to the subject matter. So these things are legal matters because they depend on the law, as in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces the chapter on prohibition (nahy) and its classification into two types. The author defines nahy linguistically as prevention and technically as a request to abandon an act from a superior. He notes that like command, the form of prohibition can be used for various meanings, but the predominant view (mukhtar) is that its effect is prohibition (tahrim). The central classification divides prohibition into: (1) prohibition of physical (hissi) acts—those whose intrinsic evil is known independently of revelation, such as adultery, drinking alcohol, lying, and injustice—and (2) prohibition of legal (shar'i) acts—acts that only take on their specific form and ruling by virtue of the law, such as fasting on Eid, selling during the call to Friday prayer, and certain acts of worship at disliked times. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if both types ultimately derive their prohibition from revelation, how can they be distinguished? The answer is clear: physical acts exist and are known as evil even without the law; their essence does not depend on the law. Legal acts, however, are literally undefined or ambiguous (mujmal) without the law—for example, 'fasting' linguistically means simply abstention, but the law adds conditions of time, intention, and purity; 'prayer' linguistically means supplication, but the law adds pillars and conditions. Therefore, legal acts have no meaningful existence without the law's specification. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi usul for understanding the scope of prohibition and the nature of acts subject to divine command.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): Linguistically prevention; technically a request to abandon an act from a superior, mainly indicating prohibition (tahrim).
الفعل الحسي (al-fi'l al-hissi): A physical act whose existence does not depend on shara'; its moral quality is known by reason or custom, e.g., adultery, theft.
الفعل الشرعي (al-fi'l al-shar'i): An act whose legal form and validity depend on shara'; without shara', it is ambiguous (mujmal) or invalid, e.g., fasting, prayer, sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a 'shar'i act' according to the classification in the chapter on prohibition?
A) Adultery (zina)
B) Lying (kadhib)
C) Fasting on the day of Eid
D) Drinking alcohol

Key Takeaway
The key distinction in the classification of prohibition is whether the act's existence depends on shara' (legal acts) or not (physical acts), not whether its ruling comes from shara'.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Definition and Classification of Prohibition (al-Nahy) in Hanafi Usul
Usul definition with critical refinement of the classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-nahy according to Hanafi usul, classify prohibitions into sensory and shar'i types, and explain why the prohibition of a shar'i act does not invalidate its essence.

Core Idea
Prohibition of a Shar'i act leaves the act valid in essence; only the attribute becomes forbidden.

The concept of prohibition (al-nahy) is central to Islamic law. In this passage, Usul al-Shashi presents a critical distinction: some prohibited acts are inherently evil and thus unlegislated, while others remain prescribed acts that become forbidden only due to an external circumstance. This lesson unpacks the definitions, the Hanafi position on the implication of nahy, and the reasoning behind the preservation of shar'i acts. You will learn how to analyse prohibition texts and apply the classification correctly.

Guided Path
1
Define al-nahy and its default implication
Identify the linguistic and technical definition of al-nahy as a demand to refrain. Note the Hanafi view that its default implication is tahrim (prohibition), and that it can be used metaphorically for other meanings (karaha, etc.) with evidence.
The commentary emphasises that the chosen view is tahrim.
2
Distinguish sensory vs. shar'i acts
Sensory acts (af'al hissiyya) are known in essence independently of Shari'ah, e.g., adultery. Shar'i acts (tasarrufat shar'iyya) depend on Shari'ah for their legal existence, e.g., prayer, fasting, sale.
The key difference is ontological: existence vs. ruling.
3
Analyse the ruling for each type
For sensory acts: the prohibited thing is the very act itself, which becomes inherently evil and thus unlegislated. For shar'i acts: the act itself is good in essence; prohibition attaches to an external attribute, so the act remains legislated but its performance becomes forbidden due to that attribute.
Examples: adultery (sensory) vs. fasting on Eid (shar'i).
4
Examine the objection and Hanafi response
An objection claims the division is unsound because both types depend on Shari'ah for their ruling. The response clarifies that the division is based on existence (wujud): sensory acts exist without Shari'ah, while shar'i acts are defined by Shari'ah and cannot exist without it.
This is a critical refinement (naqd) that solidifies the classification.
5
Apply the classification to examples
Take a hadith like 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams' (prohibition of riba). Classify it as a prohibition of a shar'i transaction. Note that its essence (sale) remains valid as a concept, but the attribute (excess) makes it invalid (batil).
The Hanafi maxim: 'Prohibition of shar'i transactions necessitates their affirmation.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصول الشاشي مع أحسن الحواشي
فصل في النهي
بقوله تعالى ولا تقربوا الزنا.
(۳)
فصل في النهي النهي نوعان: نهي عن الأفعال الحسية كالزنا وشرب الخمر والكذب والظلم ونهي عن التصرفات الشرعية كالنهي عن الصوم في يوم النحر والصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة وبيع الدرهم بالدرهمين، وحكم النوع الأول أن يكون المنهي عنه هو عين ما ورد عليه النهي فيكون عينه قبيحا فلا يكون مشروعا أصلا، وحكم النوع الثاني أن يكون المنهي عنه غير ما أضيف إليه النهي فيكون هو حسنا بنفسه قبيحا لغيره ويكون المباشر مرتكباً للحرام لغيره لا لنفسه، وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا: «النهي عن التصرفات الشرعية يقتضي تقريرها» ويراد بذلك أن التصرف بعد النهي يبقى مشروعا كما كان لأنه لو لم يبق مشروعا كان العبد عاجزاً عن فعل المنهي عنه.
(۱) قوله: [في النهي وهو المنع لغة، وفي اصطلاح أهل الأصول هو استدعاء ترك الفعل بالقول ممن هــ دونه ، وقيل : هو قول القائل لغيره لا تفعل على سبيل الاستعلاء، واختلفوا في موجبه على حـ اختلافهم في موجب الأمر لاستعماله في معان كثيرة كالتحريم والكراهة وبيان العاقبة والدعاء والشفقة والإرشاد والتحقير واليأس، لكن صيغته مجاز في غير التحريم والكراهة بالإتفاق، والمختار أنّ موجبــــه التحريم، كذا في "الفصول".
(۲) قوله: [الأفعال الحسّية] وهي التي تعرف حسنًا ولا يتوقف تحققها على الشرع كالزنا وشرب الخمر، فإنهما كانا معلومين ماهيةً قبل ورود الشرع وباقية على حالهما ولا يتغيرا بالشرع.
(۳) قوله: [كالزنا وشرب الخمر ... إلخ فإنّها أفعال حسّية يعني: يتحقق حسنًا عند من يعلم الشرع ومن لا يعلم، ولا يتوقف وجودها على الشرع، ولقائل أن يقول : لا فرق بين الأفعال الحسية والشرعية؛ لأنك إذا اعتبرت الفرق بينهما باعتبار الوجود فلا شك أنه كما لا يتوقف وجود الزنا وشرب الخمر على وجود الشرع، بل يمكن وجودهما قبل وجود الشرع، فكذا لا يتوقف وجود الصوم والبيع على ورود الشرع وإن اعتبرت الفرق بينهما باعتبار الحكم فلا شك أنه كما يتوقف حكم البيع وهو إيجاب الملك على ورود الشرع، وكذا تتوقف معرفة حكم الزنا وشرب الخمــــر وهو الحرمة ووجوب الحد على ورود الشرع أيضاً، فلا يستقيم تقسيم النهي إلى النهي عن الأفعال الحسية وإلى النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية أجيب بأنّ الفرق بينهما باعتبار الوجود، فإنّ الأفعال الحسية لا يتوقف وجودها على الشرع وإن توقف حكمها عليه، بخلاف الأفعال الشرعية حيــث يتوقف وجودها على الشرع؛ لأنها كانت محملةً عرفت ببيان الشرع ولا يمكن وجود المجمـــل إلا ببيان الشرعي فافهم كذا في "المعدن".
(٤) قوله: [كالنهي عن الصوم] فإنّ «الصوم» لغةً الإمساك، ثُمّ زيد عليه الوقت والنية والطهارة من الحيض والنفاس شرعاً، و«الصلاة» لغةً الدعاء، وزيد عليها في الشرع أشياء هي الأركان كالقيام والقراءة والركوع والسجود وشروط كالطهارة عن الحدث والخبث وستر العورة والاستقبال والنية، وكذا زيد في البيع والإجارة على المعنى اللغوي أشياء شرعيّة يرجع بعضها إلى الأهل وبعضها إلى المحل، فكانت هذه الأشياء أموراً شرعيةً؛ لأنها توقفت على الشرع، كذا في "المعدن".

فعند عدمه استحال وجودها.
(۱) قوله: [أصلاً] أي: لا ذاتاً ولا وصفاً وهذا لا خلاف فيه لأحد؛ لأنّ النهي يدلّ على القبح في المنهي عنه، فيصرفه إلى الكامل وهو ما قبح لعينه إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه فحينئذ يصير قبيحاً لمعنى في غيره، كالنهي عن الوطي في حالة الحيض وعن اتخاذ الدواب كراسيا والمشي في فعل واحد وغير ذلك من المناهي التي وردت عن الأفعال الحسية، فإنّ الدليل قد دلّ على الأفعال الحسية، فإنّ الدليل قد دلّ على النهي عنها بمعنى الأذى والشفقة لا بعين هذه الأشياء.
(۲) قوله غير ما أضيف إليه النهي كالصوم في يوم النحر منهي عنه لقوله عليه السلام: «أَلاَ لاَ تَصُومُوا في هذه الأيام فالمنهي عنه هو الإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى، فإنّ الإعراض منهي عنه لقولـه عليــــه السلام : « ومَنْ لَمْ يُحِب الدَّعْوَةَ فَقَدْ عَصَانَا».
(۳) قوله: [وعلى هذا أي على أنّ النهي عن التصرّفات الشرعية يكون حسناً بنفسه قبيحاً لغيره، قــــال أصحابنا: إنّ النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية كالصلاة والصوم والبيع وسائر العبادات والمعاملات يقع على القسم الأخير وهو الذي يكون القبح فيه لغيره متصلاً به وصفاً حتى يبقى منهي عنه بعد النهي مشروعاً بأصله عندنا وإن لم يكن مشروعاً بوصفه، فافهم.
(٤) قوله: [عاجزاً... إلخ] لأنّ التصرف الشرعي بعد النهي لو لم يبق مشروعاً لم يكن متصوّراً؛ لأنّ المتصوّر الفعل الشرعي إنما يكون من حيث الشرع وهو إنما يكون إذا كان مشروعاً فإذا لم يكن مشروعا لم يكن متصوّر الوجود و حينئذ لا يكون مقدوراً لعبد، فيكون النهي عنه عنه نهيا للعاجز و
Principles of al-Shashi with the Best Annotations
Chapter on Prohibition
By His statement, the Exalted: 'And do not approach adultery.'
(3)
Chapter on Prohibition: Prohibition is of two types: prohibition of sensory acts like adultery, drinking wine, lying, and oppression, and prohibition of Shar'i transactions like the prohibition of fasting on the day of sacrifice, prayer at disliked times, and selling a dirham for two dirhams. The ruling of the first type is that the prohibited thing is the very same thing to which the prohibition is attached, so it is inherently evil and thus not legislated at all. The ruling of the second type is that the prohibited thing is other than that to which the prohibition is attached, so it is good in itself but evil due to something else, and the doer commits a forbidden act due to something else, not due to itself. On this basis, our companions said: 'Prohibition of Shar'i transactions necessitates their affirmation,' meaning that the transaction after prohibition remains legislated as before, because if it were not legislated, the servant would be incapable of performing the prohibited act.
(1) He said: [Regarding 'prohibition': it is prevention linguistically, and in the terminology of the scholars of usul, it is a demand to refrain from an action by speech from one below him. It is also said: it is one person's statement to another 'do not do' in a superior manner. They differed regarding its implication just as they differed regarding the implication of command, because it is used for many meanings like prohibition, dislike, indicating consequence, supplication, compassion, guidance, belittlement, and despair. However, its form is metaphorical for other than prohibition and dislike by consensus, and the chosen opinion is that its implication is prohibition, as in 'al-Fusul'.
(2) He said: [Sensory acts] are those whose goodness is known and whose realization does not depend on Shari'ah, like adultery and drinking wine, for they were known in essence before the advent of Shari'ah and remain in their state and do not change by Shari'ah.
(3) He said: [Like adultery and drinking wine... etc., they are sensory acts, meaning: their goodness is realized for those who know Shari'ah and those who do not, and their existence does not depend on Shari'ah. And someone may say: There is no difference between sensory and Shar'i acts; because if you consider the difference between them with regard to existence, there is no doubt that just as the existence of adultery and drinking wine does not depend on the existence of Shari'ah—rather, they can exist before the existence of Shari'ah—so too the existence of fasting and selling does not depend on the revelation of Shari'ah. And if you consider the difference between them with regard to ruling, there is no doubt that just as the ruling of selling, which is the establishment of ownership, depends on the revelation of Shari'ah, so too the knowledge of the ruling of adultery and drinking wine—which is prohibition and the obligation of hadd—depends on the revelation of Shari'ah. So the division of prohibition into prohibition of sensory acts and prohibition of Shar'i acts is not sound. It was answered that the difference between them is with regard to existence, for sensory acts do not depend on Shari'ah for their existence even if their ruling depends on it, unlike Shar'i acts where their existence depends on Shari'ah because they were summary [mujmal] and became known through the explanation of Shari'ah, and the summary cannot exist except by the explanation of the Shari'ah; so understand, as in 'al-Ma'din'.
(4) He said: [Like the prohibition of fasting] for 'fasting' linguistically is abstention, then time, intention, and purity from menstruation and postnatal bleeding were added by Shari'ah. And 'prayer' linguistically is supplication, and things were added by Shari'ah: the pillars like standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration, and conditions like purification from hadath and impurity, covering of the 'awrah, facing the qiblah, and intention. Similarly, in selling and hiring, things of a Shar'i nature were added to the linguistic meaning, some pertaining to the capacity and some to the object. So these things are Shar'i matters because they depend on Shari'ah, as in 'al-Ma'din'.

By his statement, peace be upon him: 'Do not fast on these days.' i.e., the times of sunset and noon.
Chapter on Prohibition
By his statement, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams.'
The day of sacrifice and prayer at disliked times and selling a dirham for two dirhams, and the ruling—that is, the prohibition of sensory acts.
Regarding it
The first type: that the prohibited thing is the very thing to which the prohibition is attached, so it is the very same as the prohibited thing—that is, the prohibition of Shar'i acts—the very same as the prohibited thing, evil, so it is not legislated at all. And the ruling of the second type: that the prohibited thing is other than that to which the prohibition is attached, so it is good in itself, evil due to something else, and the doer commits a forbidden act due to something else, not due to itself. On this basis, our companions said: 'Prohibition concerning the universal principle,' i.e., their statement that prohibition of Shar'i transactions necessitates their affirmation, meaning that the transaction after the prohibition remains legislated as before, because if it were not legislated, the servant would be incapable of performing the prohibited act—because their existence is by the existence of Shari'ah's consideration, so when it is absent, their existence is impossible.
(1) He said: [At all] i.e., neither in essence nor in attribute, and there is no disagreement about this, because prohibition indicates evil in the prohibited thing, so it turns it to the complete, which is what is evil in itself, unless evidence indicates otherwise; then it becomes evil due to a meaning in something else, like the prohibition of intercourse during menstruation, making animals into chairs, walking in one action, and other prohibitions that have come regarding sensory acts, for evidence has indicated that the prohibition of them is in the sense of harm and compassion, not of these things themselves.
(2) He said: Other than that to which the prohibition is attached, like fasting on the day of sacrifice is prohibited because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, do not fast on these days.' The prohibited thing is turning away from the hospitality of Allah Almighty, for turning away is prohibited by his statement, peace be upon him: 'And whoever does not love the invitation has disobeyed us.'
(3) He said: [And on this basis], i.e., that the prohibition of Shar'i transactions is good in itself, evil due to something else, our companions said: The prohibition of Shar'i acts like prayer, fasting, selling, and all acts of worship and transactions falls into the last category, which is that the evil is due to something else attached to it as an attribute, so that after the prohibition it remains legislated in its essence according to us, even if not legislated in its attribute; so understand.
(4) He said: [Incapable... etc.] Because the Shar'i act after the prohibition, if it were not legislated, would not be conceivable; for the conceivable Shar'i act is only from the perspective of Shari'ah, and it is only if it is legislated. So if it is not legislated, it is not conceivable of existence, and then it is not within the servant's power, so prohibiting it would be a prohibition of the incapable.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines prohibition (al-nahy) and its two types. The matn states that prohibition is of two kinds: (1) prohibition of sensory acts (af'al hissiyya) like adultery, wine, lying, and oppression; and (2) prohibition of Shar'i transactions (tasarrufat shar'iyya) like fasting on Eid al-Adha, prayer at disliked times, and selling a dirham for two. The first type renders the act inherently evil and thus unlegislated. The second type leaves the act good in itself but evil due to an external attribute, so the doer commits a forbidden act not because the act is evil in itself but because of something attached. The Hanafi school then states that prohibition of Shar'i transactions actually confirms their legitimacy, as they remain legislated after the prohibition; otherwise, the servant would be incapable of performing them, making the prohibition void. The commentary provides detailed analysis: the definition of nahy, the distinction between sensory and Shar'i acts, and a refutation of an objection that the division is unsound. The objection argues that both types depend on Shari'ah for their ruling, but the answer clarifies that sensory acts exist without Shari'ah, while Shar'i acts require Shari'ah for their very existence, as they are summary concepts (mujmal) that become detailed by Shari'ah. Examples: fasting initially means abstention, but Shari'ah adds time, intention, purity; similarly, prayer adds pillars and conditions.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): Linguistically prevention; in usul, a demand to refrain from an action by speech from a superior to an inferior. Its default implication is prohibition.
الأفعال الحسية (al-af'al al-hissiyya): Sensory acts whose goodness or badness is known intellectually and whose existence does not depend on Shari'ah (e.g., adultery, murder). Their rulings (e.g., prohibition) are determined by Shari'ah but their essence is known prior.
التصرفات الشرعية (al-tasarrufat al-shar'iyya): Shar'i transactions whose existence depends on Shari'ah because they are defined by Shari'ah (e.g., prayer, fasting, sale). They are good in themselves (hasan li-dhatih) but may be prohibited due to an external attribute (qabih li-ghayrih).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the prohibition of sensory acts and the prohibition of Shar'i acts?
A) Sensory acts are prohibited because they are evil in essence; Shar'i acts are prohibited because they are evil in essence as well.
B) Sensory acts become evil in themselves due to the prohibition; Shar'i acts are evil in themselves but may become permissible with evidence.
C) Sensory acts become evil in themselves due to the prohibition; Shar'i acts are good in themselves but become evil due to an external attribute, and they remain valid in essence.
D) Both types of acts remain valid in essence; only the performance is prohibited.

Key Takeaway
Prohibition is of two types: sensory acts become inherently evil and unlegislated; Shar'i acts remain good in essence and valid as acts, but become forbidden due to an external attribute.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Types of Prohibition and Hanafi Doctrine on Shar'i Acts
Explaining two types of prohibition and the Hanafi rule that prohibition of Shar'i acts implies their continued validity in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of prohibition (inherent vs. external) and apply the Hanafi rule that prohibition of Shar'i acts does not vitiate their essential validity.

Core Idea
Prohibition of a Shar'i act invalidates its description, not its essence; the act remains legally valid.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification of prohibitions. The source text from Usul al-Shashi explains how a prohibition that attaches to an act for an external reason (qabih li-ghayrihi) leaves the act's essential validity intact. This principle has profound implications for transactions, rituals, and vows, as illustrated by the examples of defective sale and fasting on prohibited days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of prohibition
Distinguish between inherent prohibition (qabih li-aynihi) where the act itself is evil, and external prohibition (qabih li-ghayrihi) where the act is good in itself but becomes evil due to an external factor.
The Hanafi classification is key to understanding the effect of prohibitions on Shar'i acts.
2
Understand the Hanafi principle
Grasp the rule: 'النهي عن التصرفات الشرعية يقتضي تقريرها' – prohibition of Shar'i acts necessitates their establishment (i.e., they remain valid in essence).
This is based on the absurdity of prohibiting an act the servant cannot perform if it were invalidated.
3
Examine the rationale
If the act became entirely invalid, the servant would be incapable of performing it, and prohibiting the impossible is absurd. Therefore, the act must remain legally conceivable.
Contrast with physical acts (al-af'al al-hissiyyah) whose existence does not depend on legislation.
4
Apply the principle to examples
Apply the rule to defective sale (ownership transfers), defective lease, vow to fast on Eid (valid in essence), and all prohibited Shar'i acts.
Note the Shafi'i disagreement on the vow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النهي بقوله عليه السلام لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين. يوم النحر والصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة وبيع الدرهم بالدرهمين، وحكم النهي عن الأفعال الحسية. النوع الأول أن يكون المنهي عنه هو عين ما ورد عليه النهي فيكون عين المنهي عنه قبيحا فلا يكون مشروعا أصلا، وحكم النوع الثاني أن يكون المنهي عنه غير ما أضيف إليه النهي فيكون هو حسنا بنفسه قبيحا لغيره ويكون المباشر مرتكباً للحرام لغيره لا لنفسه، وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا: «النهي عن التصرفات الشرعية يقتضي تقريرها ويراد بذلك أن التصرف بعد النهي يبقى مشروعا كما كان لأنه لو لم يبق مشروعا كان العبد عاجزاً عن تحصيل المشروع وحينئذ كان ذلك نهيا للعاجز وذلك من الشارع محال وبه فارق الأفعال الحسية؛ لأنه لو كان عينها قبيحاً لا يؤدي ذلك إلى نهي العاجز؛ لأنه بهذا الوصف لا يعجز العبد عن الفعل الحسي، ويتفرع من هذا بقاء التصرفات بعد النهي مشروعة. فقلنا: البيع الفاسد يفيد الملك عندنا، وهذا حكم البيع الفاسد والإجارة الفاسدة والنذر بصوم يوم النحر وجميع صور التصرفات الشرعية مع ورود النهي عنها.

(1) قوله: [أصلاً] أي: لا ذاتاً ولا وصفاً وهذا لا خلاف فيه لأحد؛ لأنّ النهي يدلّ على القبح في المنهي عنه، فيصرفه إلى الكامل وهو ما قبح لعينه إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه فحينئذ يصير قبيحاً لمعنى في غيره، كالنهي عن الوطي في حالة الحيض وعن اتخاذ الدواب كراسيا والمشي في فعل واحد وغير ذلك من المناهي التي وردت عن الأفعال الحسية، فإنّ الدليل قد دلّ على النهي عنها بمعنى الأذى والشفقة لا بعين هذه الأشياء.
(2) قوله غير ما أضيف إليه النهي كالصوم في يوم النحر منهي عنه لقوله عليه السلام: «أَلاَ لاَ تَصُومُوا في هذه الأيام فالمنهي عنه هو الإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى، فإنّ الإعراض منهي عنه لقولـه عليه السلام : « ومَنْ لَمْ يُحِب الدَّعْوَةَ فَقَدْ عَصَانَا».
(3) قوله: [وعلى هذا أي على أنّ النهي عن التصرّفات الشرعية يكون حسناً بنفسه قبيحاً لغيره، قال أصحابنا: إنّ النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية كالصلاة والصوم والبيع وسائر العبادات والمعاملات يقع على القسم الأخير وهو الذي يكون القبح فيه لغيره متصلاً به وصفاً حتى يبقى منهي عنه بعد النهي مشروعاً بأصله عندنا وإن لم يكن مشروعاً بوصفه، فافهم.
(4) قوله: [عاجزاً... إلخ] لأنّ التصرف الشرعي بعد النهي لو لم يبق مشروعاً لم يكن متصوّراً؛ لأنّ المتصوّر الفعل الشرعي إنما يكون من حيث الشرع وهو إنما يكون إذا كان مشروعاً فإذا لم يكن مشروعا لم يكن متصوّر الوجود و حينئذ لا يكون مقدوراً لعبد، فيكون النهي عنه نهيا للعاجز، ونهي العاجز قبيح، وهو من الشارع محال؛ لأنه بمنزلة قول القائل للأعمى: «لا تبصر»، وللأمي: «لا تقرأ» فهو قبيح، فكذا هذا بخلاف الحسي؛ لأنّ تصوّره لا يعتمد المشروعية. (1) [لا يعجز العبد عن الفعل] لأنّ تصوّره لا يعتمد المشروعيّة؛ لأنه أمكن تحقق هذه الأفعال مع صفة القبح؛ لأنها توجد حسا فلا يمتنع وجودها بسبب القبح، والمراد بالأفعال الحسية ما لا يتوقف تحققها على الشرع، وقيل: ما يكون معانيها المعلومة قبل الشرع باقية على حالها بعد ورود الشرع لم يتغيّر به أصلاً كالزنا والقتل وشرب الخمر وغير ذلك.
(2) [حكم البيع الفاسد] بأن باع عبداً بشرط أن يخدم البائع شهراً وهو منهي عنه بما روي أنه عليه السلام: «نَهَى عَنْ بَيْعٍ وَشَرْط»، وغير ذلك، فإن النهي فيه ورد لمعنى في غير البيع وهـو الفضل الخالي عن العوض والشرط الفاسد فلا ينعدم به أصل المشروع؛ لأنه إيجاب وقبول من أهله في محلّه.
(3) [والنذر بصوم يوم النحر ولقائل أن يقول: «حق الكلام أن يقول: وصوم يوم النحر»؛ لأنّ الكلام حينئذ في نظائر النواهي التي وردت على التصرّفات المشروعة التي هي مشروعة بأصلها غير مشروعة بوصفها، وصوم يوم النحر هو المشروع بأصله بوجود ركنه نهاراً مع النية، وإنما الفساد في الوصف وهو الإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى، وأما النذر فهو صحيح عندنا من كلّ وجه، ولا يصح عند الشافعي رحمه الله فلا يصح نظيراً، اللهم إلا أن يقال: النذر بصوم يوم النحر حكم الصوم فيصلح نظيراً على طريق التسامح؛ لأن صحته مبني على صحة الصوم على الاختلاف.
(4) [يفيد الملك... إلخ] لأنه إيجاب وقبول صدر من أهله في محلّه، ولا يختلّ من ذلك بشرط فاسد فكان أمراً زائداً على العقد، فكان البيع الفاسد عقداً شرعيا بأصله فكان مفيداً للملك، لكن لما كان حراماً شرعاً كان واجب النقض حقا للشرع.
Chapter on Prohibition: By his statement, peace be upon him, 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams.' The day of sacrifice, prayer at disliked times, and selling a dirham for two dirhams, and the ruling of prohibition concerning physical acts. The first type: that the prohibited thing is the very same thing upon which the prohibition fell, so it is inherently bad and not legislated at all. The ruling of the second type: that the prohibited thing is other than that to which the prohibition is attached, so it is good in itself, bad due to something else, and the doer commits a forbidden act due to something else, not due to itself. Upon this, our companions said: 'Prohibition of Shar'i acts necessitates their establishment, meaning that the act after prohibition remains legislated as it was, because if it did not remain legislated, the servant would be incapable of achieving the legislated act, and that would be a prohibition of the incapable, and that is impossible from the Lawgiver, and by this it differs from physical acts; because if it were inherently bad, that would not lead to prohibition of the incapable, because by this description the servant is not incapable of the physical act.' From this branches the remaining of Shar'i acts after prohibition as legislated. So we said: The defective sale confers ownership according to us, and this is the ruling of the defective sale, defective lease, the vow to fast on the day of sacrifice, and all forms of Shar'i acts when prohibition is attached to them.

(1) His saying: [Essentially] meaning neither in essence nor attribute, and there is no disagreement on this; because prohibition indicates badness in the prohibited, so it diverts it to perfection, that which is bad in itself, unless evidence indicates otherwise, then it becomes bad due to a meaning in something else, like the prohibition of intercourse during menstruation, taking animals as chairs, walking in one act, and other prohibitions reported concerning physical acts. Evidence has indicated that these are prohibited due to harm and compassion, not due to the essence of these things.
(2) His saying: other than that to which the prohibition is attached, like fasting on the day of sacrifice is prohibited by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not fast on these days.' The prohibited is the turning away from the hospitality of Allah Almighty, because turning away is prohibited by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever does not love the invitation has disobeyed us.'
(3) His saying: [And upon this] meaning that the prohibition of Shar'i acts is good in itself, bad due to something else. Our companions said: 'Indeed, the prohibition of Shar'i acts like prayer, fasting, sale, and all acts of worship and transactions falls under the last category, which is where the badness is due to something else attached as a description, so that it remains prohibited after the prohibition but legislated in its essence according to us, even if not legislated in its description.' So understand.
(4) His saying: [Incapable... etc.] because the Shar'i act after prohibition, if it did not remain legislated, it would not be conceivable; because the conceivable Shar'i act exists only by virtue of the Law, and it exists only if it is legislated; if it is not legislated, its existence is inconceivable, and then it is not within the servant's capacity, so the prohibition would be a prohibition of the incapable. Prohibition of the incapable is bad, and that is impossible from the Lawgiver, because it is like someone saying to a blind person: 'Do not see,' or to an illiterate person: 'Do not read,' which is bad. So this is similar, unlike the physical act, because its conception does not depend on legislation. (1) [The servant is not incapable of the act] because its conception does not depend on legislation; because it is possible for these acts to occur with the attribute of badness, since they exist physically, so their existence is not prevented by badness. Physical acts are those whose realization does not depend on the Law, or whose known meanings before the Law remain as they are after the Law without change, like adultery, murder, drinking wine, etc.
(2) [Ruling of the defective sale]: such as selling a slave with the condition that he serve the seller for a month, which is prohibited by the reported his statement, peace be upon him: 'He prohibited a sale with a condition,' and others. The prohibition in this came due to a meaning in other than the sale, which is the excess free of consideration and the invalid condition. Therefore, the basis of legislation is not nullified, because it is an offer and acceptance from qualified parties in its place.
(3) [And the vow to fast on the day of sacrifice]: one might say: 'The proper statement is: and fasting the day of sacrifice; because the discussion concerns analogous prohibitions that fell on legislated acts which are legislated in their essence but not legislated in their description. Fasting on the day of sacrifice is legislated in its essence by the existence of its pillar (fasting during the day with intention), but the corruption lies in the description, which is turning away from the hospitality of Allah. As for the vow, it is valid from every aspect according to us, but it is not valid according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, so it is not a valid analogy. Unless it is said that the vow to fast on the day of sacrifice has the same ruling as the fast itself, so it is suitable as an analogy by way of leniency, because its validity depends on the validity of the fast according to the disagreement.
(4) [Confers ownership... etc.] because it is an offer and acceptance from qualified parties in its place, and an invalid condition does not impair that; it is something additional to the contract. So the defective sale is a Shar'i contract in its essence, thus it confers ownership. But since it is forbidden by law, it must be rescinded as a right of the Law.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi with its commentary (Ahsan al-Hawashi) examines the legal nature of prohibitions. It opens by defining two categories of prohibitions: (1) those where the prohibited act is inherently evil, making the act entirely invalid; (2) those where the act is good in itself but becomes evil due to an external factor, such as fasting on 'Eid al-Adha—the fast itself is valid, but one is prohibited because it turns away from God's hospitality. The Hanafi school then applies the second category to all Shar'i acts (like prayer, fasting, sales, and other transactions): a prohibition nullifies the act's description but not its essence. This is because if the act lost all legal validity, the servant would be incapable of performing it, and prohibiting an impossible act is morally absurd (like telling a blind person not to see). Thus, prohibited Shar'i acts remain legally conceivable and valid in essence. The commentary illustrates this with examples: a defective sale (e.g., selling a slave with a condition of service) still transfers ownership because the contract's essence (offer and acceptance) is intact; a vow to fast on a prohibited day is valid in itself because the act of fasting is sound in essence, despite the prohibition. The passage also contrasts with the Shafi'i position on the validity of such vows. The Hanafi principle ensures that believers remain capable of performing the basic acts, even when they bear a prohibited attribute.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahyu): طلب الكف عن الفعل على وجه الاستعلاء
المنهي عنه (al-manhiyyu anhu): الفعل الذي ورد النهي عنه
الأفعال الحسية (al-af'alu al-hissiyyah): الأفعال التي توجد حساً ولا تتوقف على الشّرع، كالزنا والقتل
الأفعال الشرعية (al-af'alu al-shar'iyyah): الأفعال التي تتوقف صحتها على اعتبار الشرع، كالصلاة والبيع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in this passage, what is the ruling on a defective sale (al-bay' al-fasid) when the prohibition is due to an invalid condition?
A) The sale is entirely void and no ownership is transferred.
B) The sale is valid in essence and transfers ownership, but must be rescinded because it is forbidden.
C) The sale is valid in every aspect because the condition is irrelevant.
D) The sale is valid only if the condition is removed before the transaction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that prohibition of a Shar'i act (like sale, prayer, fasting) only affects its description, not its essence; the act remains legally valid and conceivable.

Ruling on Starting Nafl Prayer During Prohibited Times and Comparison with Fasting on Eid
Explaining the ruling on commencing nafl worship in prohibited times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that commencement of a nafl prayer makes completion necessary (luzum) and distinguish it from the case of nafl fasting on Eid day.

Core Idea
Starting a nafl prayer in prohibited time binds you to complete it; not so for fasting on Eid.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key usul principle: once you start a nafl prayer even in a prohibited time, it becomes binding to complete it. However, this does not apply to all acts of worship. The passage contrasts prayer with fasting on Eid al-Adha (day of Nahr), where starting a nafl fast does not bind you to finish it. This difference illustrates how the Hanafi school considers the nature of the act and its relation to time. You will also encounter an intra-madhhab disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad on one side, and Imam Abu Yusuf on the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Determine what the act is (nafl prayer) and its ruling when performed in prohibited times (makruh tahrimi).
Prohibited times for prayer: sunrise, zenith, sunset.
2
State the rule of luzum upon commencement
Despite the initial prohibition, once you start the prayer, it becomes wajib to complete it. This is because the prohibition is not intrinsic to the prayer itself but to the time.
This is based on the Hanafi principle that a nafl act becomes binding once started if it can be completed without ongoing prohibition.
3
Distinguish prayer from fasting on Eid
Contrast with fasting on the day of Nahr: starting a nafl fast does not bind completion because fasting is measured by the day, and the entire day is prohibited, so completing would necessarily involve a prohibited act.
Prayer can be completed after the time passes and is not intrinsically tied to the time.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Note the disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that no qada is required if the fast is broken, while Abu Yusuf analogizes to prayer and requires qada.
The text prefers the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad as the Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon opinion is that completion and qada are not required for a nafl fast on Eid, due to the impossibility of completing without dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النهي أي الشارع في النفل. ١٢ الأوقات لزمه بالشروع، وارتكاب الحرام ليس بلازم للزوم الإتمام فإنه لو ومكث بعد الشرع. ۱۲ أي الشارع في النفل فيها. ١٢ بيان حل الصلاة. ۱۲ أي الشمس. ١.٢ أي الشارع في النفل. ١٢ صبر حتى حلت الصلاة بارتفاع الشمس وغروبها ودلوكها أمكنه الإتمام أي جازت بلا كراهة ١٢ فلا يكون ارتكاب الحرام لازما. ١٢ (۲) النفل. ١٢ وهو يوم النحر . ١٢ أي زوالها نقطة نصف النهار. ١٢ الشارع في صوم يوم النحر ١٢٠٠ بدون الكراهة وبه فارق صوم يوم العيد فإنه لو شرع فيه لا يلزمه عند أبي أي بعدم لزوم الارتكاب للحرام. ١٢ أي إتمام صوم يوم النحر. ١٢ حنيفة ومحمد عليهما الرحمة؛ لأن الإتمام لا ينفك عن ارتكاب الحرام. ١٢ (۲) قوله: [وبه فارق ... إلخ ] أي: بما ذكرنا أنه يمكن الإتمام في الصلاة بدون الكراهـة فــارق الشروع في الصلاة في هذه الأوقات من الشروع في صوم يوم النحر، فإنّه إذا شرع في الصوم في يوم النحر لايلزمه الإتمام وإذا أفسده لا يلزمه القضاء في "ظاهر الرواية"، وعن أبي يوسف رحمه الله أنه يلزمه القضاء والإتمام في الصوم كما في الصلاة، وجه الفرق أنّ الصوم مقــدر باليوم فلا يمكنه الإتمام بدون الإعراض عن الأكل والشرب في ذلك اليوم وهو مكروه فلا يمكنه الإتمام بدون الكراهة بخلاف الصلاة، فإنّها لا توجد بالوقت ؛ لأنه ظرفها والظرف لا تاثير له في إيجاد المظروف، بل الصلاة توجد بأفعال معلومة والوقت مجاور لها، فلا يمكن فساد الوقت مؤثراً في فسادها فتدبّر، كذا في "المعدن". ١٢
Chapter on Prohibition. He means the one who starts a supererogatory prayer. At the times, it becomes binding upon starting, and committing the prohibited [act] is not necessary due to the necessity of completion. Indeed, if he waits after starting until the prayer becomes permissible by the rising of the sun, its setting, or its declination, he can complete it, meaning it is permitted without dislike, so committing the prohibited act is not necessary. And by this it differs from fasting on the day of Nahr [Eid al-Adha]; if one starts fasting on that day, it is not binding upon him according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad - may mercy be upon them - because completion cannot be separated from committing the prohibited act. The statement: [And by this it differs... etc.] means: by what we mentioned that completion in prayer without dislike is possible, the commencement of prayer in these times differs from the commencement of fasting on the day of Nahr. For if one starts fasting on the day of Nahr, completion is not binding upon him, and if he invalidates it, qada is not obligatory in Zahir al-Riwayah. And from Abu Yusuf - may Allah have mercy on him - that qada and completion in fasting are obligatory as in prayer. The point of difference is that fasting is measured by the day, so he cannot complete it without refraining from eating and drinking on that day, which is disliked, so he cannot complete without dislike. Unlike prayer, for it does not exist by the time, because time is its container and the container has no effect in bringing about the contained; rather, prayer exists by known actions and time is adjacent to it, so corruption of time cannot be effective in corrupting it. Ponder. Thus in al-Ma'dan.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter on prohibition. The author explains the rule: if one begins a nafl (supererogatory) prayer during a prohibited time (e.g., at sunrise, sunset, or noon), it becomes necessary to complete it. This is because the prohibition of praying at such times is not absolute; once started, the obligation to complete the prayer overrides the initial dislike. The commentator clarifies that the act of praying during these times does not necessarily involve committing a prohibited act, because one can complete the prayer after the time becomes permissible (e.g., after sunrise) without dislike. This is contrasted with fasting on the day of Nahr (Eid al-Adha). If someone starts a nafl fast on that day, the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule that it is not binding to complete it, and if broken, no qada is required. The reason is that the entire day is prohibited for fasting, so completing the fast inevitably involves continuing a prohibited act. Abu Yusuf holds that completion and qada are required, analogizing to prayer. The commentator explains the distinction: prayer is not intrinsically connected to its time; time is merely its container and does not affect the validity of the prayer. Fasting, however, is defined by the day, so abstaining for the whole day is essential. This analysis highlights how different acts interact with prohibitions. The text draws on "Zahir al-Riwayah" (the relied-upon transmission) and cites the work "al-Ma'dan."

Key Terms
نهي (nahy): Prohibition from the Lawgiver, which can be for an intrinsic or extrinsic reason.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory act of worship, not obligatory but recommended.
لزوم (luzum): Necessity or obligation arising from the commencement of an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does starting a nafl prayer during a prohibited time still make completion binding, while starting a nafl fast on the day of Nahr does not?
A) Because prayer is more important than fasting.
B) Because prayer can be completed without dislike after the time passes, but fasting cannot avoid dislike throughout the day.
C) Because Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagreed with Abu Yusuf.
D) Because the prohibition on prayer is weaker than the prohibition on fasting.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that commencement of a nafl prayer in a prohibited time makes completion binding because the prohibition is not intrinsic, unlike a nafl fast on Eid where the entire day is prohibited and completion cannot avoid dislike.

Legal Effects of Prohibited Acts: Prohibition Does Not Negate Rulings
Establishes that prohibition does not negate legal consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that prohibition does not negate legal consequences and apply it to examples like divorce during menses and ablution with usurped water.

Core Idea
A prohibited act can still be valid in its legal effects — the sin is on the doer, not the act itself.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi usul principle: that the prohibition (hurmat) of an act does not prevent it from having its normal legal consequences. You will see how this principle applies to cases like divorce during menstruation and ablution with usurped water, and how it distinguishes the Hanafi school from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the core rule: prohibition does not negate the attachment of rulings (tarattub al-ahkam). The Arabic phrase 'hurmat al-fi'l la tunafi tarattub al-ahkam' is the key.
2
Examine the Examples
Look at divorce during menstruation and ablution with usurped water. These acts are prohibited but still valid and produce their usual rulings: divorce counts, ablution purifies.
The prohibition is for an external reason (e.g., time, property), not due to the act's essence.
3
Apply to Intercourse Case
Consider intercourse with a menstruating woman: it is haram, yet it still establishes ihsan, makes a thrice-divorced woman permissible to her first husband, and requires mahr, 'idda, and maintenance.
The text states 'yatarattabu al-ahkam 'ala hadha al-wati''.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Observe the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) regarding maintenance when a woman refuses due to dower. Even here, the principle stands: prohibition does not negate validity; the dispute is about a separate condition.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i
Understand that Shafi'is hold a prohibited act cannot be valid for legal benefits, while Hanafis distinguish between prohibition and validity.
This principle has widespread application across fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحرمة الفعل لا تنافي ترتب الأحكام كطلاق الحائض والوضوء بالمياه المغصوبة وإن كان ممنوعا فإنه يقع. ولهذا قلنا: يترتب الأحكام على هذا الوطئ فيثبت به إحصان الواطئ وتحل المرأة للزوج الأول ويثبت به حكم المهر والعدة والنفقة، ولو امتنعت عن التمكين لأجل الصداق لا تستحق النفقة والسكني عند أبي حنيفة تستحق النفقة. كان ناشزة عندهما فلا تستحق النفقة، وحرمة الفعل لا تنافي ترتب أحكام الوجود والصحة.
And the prohibition of an act does not contradict the attachment of rulings, like divorce during menstruation and ablution with usurped water, even though it is prohibited, it occurs. And because of this we say: rulings are attached to this intercourse, for it establishes the ihsan of the man, makes the woman permissible to the first husband, and establishes the rulings of dower, waiting period, and maintenance. And if she refuses to grant access due to the dower, she does not deserve maintenance and housing; according to Abu Hanifa, she deserves maintenance. She was rebellious according to the two, so she does not deserve maintenance. And the prohibition of the act does not contradict the attachment of rulings of existence and validity.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage articulates a key Hanafi usul principle: the prohibition (haramah) of an act does not prevent it from having legal consequences. The examples given are: (1) Divorce pronounced during a woman's menstruation is sinful but valid; it still effects divorce. (2) Ablution with usurped water is a sin, but it still purifies. (3) Intercourse with a menstruating woman, though prohibited, still establishes ihsan (making the man muhsan), makes a thrice-divorced woman permissible to her first husband (if she remarried), and requires payment of mahr, observance of 'idda, and provision of maintenance. The passage also addresses a subsidiary issue: if a woman refuses to let her husband have intercourse because of delayed dower, according to Abu Hanifa she still deserves maintenance, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, she does not (she is considered rebellious). The core principle remains: the prohibition does not negate the legal effects of an act that has been performed. This is in contrast to the Shafi'i school, which holds that a prohibited act cannot be a valid cause for reward or legal benefit. The Hanafi position is based on the idea that the act itself is not intrinsically void; the prohibition is an external matter.

Key Terms
حرمة الفعل (hurmat al-fi'l): The legal prohibition of an act; its sinfulness.
ترتب الأحكام (tarattub al-ahkam): The attachment of legal rulings to an act despite prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Prayer performed in a stolen garment is invalid.

C) Fasting on the day of Eid is mandatory once started.
D) All prohibited acts are void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a prohibited act, unless declared void by explicit text, still produces its usual legal effects.

The Principle that External Prohibition Does Not Nullify Validity
Usul rule with dalil and application to testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that prohibition due to external factors does not negate the fundamental legality (mashru'iyya) of an act, and derive the ruling on marriage with fasig witnesses using the Quranic verse on testimony.

Core Idea
Prohibition of an act affirms its existence and does not invalidate its essence.

This passage from al-Shashi establishes a key principle in Hanafi usul: a prohibition (nahy) that is attached to an external circumstance—such as using usurped property or selling at the time of the call to prayer—does not nullify the fundamental validity of the act. The act remains legally effective, though sinful. The author then applies this principle to the Quranic prohibition on accepting testimony from slanderers, arguing that such individuals retain their eligibility to testify (ahl al-shahada), so a marriage witnessed by them is valid. This lesson unpacks the principle, the evidence, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Read the opening lines: 'And if hunting with it is forbidden, yet the game becomes permissible.' The author asserts that despite prohibition, the intended legal effect (halal game, ownership) follows. This is the principle: external nahy does not invalidate mashru'iyya.
Focus on the distinction between essence and attribute.
2
Examine the Four Examples
The text gives four cases: (1) hunting with a usurped bow, (2) slaughtering with a usurped knife, (3) praying on usurped land, (4) selling during the Friday call. For each, note the prohibited element (usurpation, time) and the valid legal effect (halal meat, valid prayer, ownership transfer).
The prohibition is on the external factor, not the act's essence.
3
Analyze the Quranic Dalil (al-Nur: 4)
The verse 'And never accept their testimony' is cited. The author argues that the prohibition of accepting testimony implies that the testimony exists and comes from eligible people. Otherwise, the prohibition would be meaningless. This supports the principle.
The dalil is Quranic, but the dalala (indication) is zanni—requiring interpretation.
4
Apply to Marriage with Fasig Witnesses
The text concludes that the fasiq is still from ahl al-shahada (people of testimony). Therefore, if a marriage is contracted in the presence of two fasig witnesses, the marriage is valid. The judge must uphold it if the couple comes before him.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference from schools that would invalidate such a marriage.
5
Understand the Objection and Response
An objection is raised: if they are eligible, why is their testimony not accepted? The answer: they remain eligible, but their testimony is rejected due to a defect in performance (fasad fi al-ada), not lack of capacity. This is consistent with the principle.
The objection is addressed through the distinction between eligibility (ahliyya) and acceptance (qubul).

وإن كان الاصطياد بها حراما لكن يحل الصيد. ١٢.

والاصطياد بقوس مغصوبة والذبح بسكين مغصوبة والصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة والبيع في وقت النداء فإنّه يترتب الحكم على هذه التصرفات مع اشتمالها على الحرمة، وباعتبار هذا الأصل قلنا في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا شَهَادَةً أَبَداً [النور: ٤]، إن الفاسق من أهل الشهادة فينعقد النكاح بشهادة الفساق. ١٢

(٦) قوله: [إنّ الفاسق من أهل الشهادة أورد عليه أن تفريع هذه المسألة على أنّ النهي يقتضي بقاء المشروعية مشكل، فإنّ المنهي عنه هو قبول الشهادة، فهذا الأصل يقتضي أن يكون قبول شهادتهم مشروعاً بأصله غير مشروع بوصفه، فينبغي أن ينعقد قبولها لثبوت حق الغير له، إما أن يكون قبولهـــا باطلاً من كُلّ، ولا يكون شهادة بأصلها فليس من مقتضيات هذا الأصل، وأجيب عنه: بأن قبول الشهادة وعدَمه يستلزم أهليّة الشهادة فكان النهي عن قبول الشهادة نهيا عن أهليتها وهو يقتضي تقرير أهليته للشهادة فيكون هذه المسألة من مقتضيات هذا الأصل بهذا الطريق، كذا في "الملتقط". ١٢

أصول الشاشي مع أحسن الحواشي
فصل في النهي
أي جنسهم فيعم الإثنين. ١٢ بشهادة الفساق ؛ لأن النهى عن قبول الشهادة بدون الشهادة محال، وإنّما لم تقبل شهادتهم لفساد في الأداء لا لعدم الشهادة أصلا وعلى هذا لا يجب عليهم اللعان؛ لأن ذلك أداء الشهادة ولا أداء مع الفسق لأنه فيه لحملها، وهو خمس شهادات خامستها اللعنة أو الغضب. من أهلها؛ لأنهما يقدران أيضاً على الإخبار عن علمهما، وأجيب بأنّ هذا مسلم إلا أنّ أهلية الشهادة في الشاهد شرط إجماعاً؛ لأنّ الشهادة نفاد القول على الغير وهو غير متصوّر إلا لمن له الولاية وذا بالأهلية وليست في الصبي والعبد بخلاف الفاسق، فإنّها موجودة ولأنها بالتكليف في الحرية وقد تحققا في الفاسق. ١٢
(۱) قوله: [بشهادة الفساق حتى لو تزوّج رجل امرأةً بحضور شاهدين فاسقين يقرر ذلك النكاح شرعاً حتى لو أتى العاقدان عند القاضي وأخبرا بالنكاح بشهادتهما وجب على القاضي أن يقرر ذلك النكاح؛ لأنهما من أهل الشهادة بمقتضى النهي بناء على أن النهي من الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي بقاء المشروعية؛ لأنّ النهي عن قبول الشهادة بدون الشهادة محال؛ لأنّ قبول الشيء وعدمه إنما يتصوّر بعد وجود ذلك الشيء، فثبت أنه من أهل الشهادة. ١٢ (۲) قوله: [ بدون الشهادة محال؛ لأنه لو لم يكن شهادتهم شهادةً لا يتصوّر النهي عن قبولها، ثُمّ يرد عليه أنّ الفساق لَمّا كانوا من أهل الشهادة فينبغي أن تقبل شهادتهم، فأجاب بقوله: «وإنّما لم تقبل... إلخ » كذا في "المعدن". ۱۲
(۳) قوله: [اللعان] بيانه إذا قذف الرجل امرأته بالزنا وهما من أهل الشهادة والمرأة ممن يحدّ قاذفها بأن كانت عفيفة من الزنا فطالبته بموجب القذف، فعليه اللعان وهو في اللغة الطرد والبعد، وفي الشرع هي شهادات مؤكدات بالأيمان مقرونة باللعن، وفي الغضب قائمة مقام حد القذف في حقه ومقام حد الزنا في حقها، وصفة اللعان أن يبتدئ القاضي بالزوج فيشهد أربع شهادات، ويقول في كُلّ مرة: «أشهد بالله إني لمن الصادقين فيما رميتها به من الزنا» يشير إليها في جميع ذلك، ويقول في الخامسة: «لعنة الله عليه إن كان الكاذبين»، ثُمّ تشهد المرأة أربع مرات تقول في كُلّ مرّة: «أشهد بالله إنه لمن الكاذبين فيما رماني به من الزنا وتقول في الخامس: «غضب الله عليها إن كان من الصادقين فيما رماني به من الزنا»، كذا قيل. ١٢

And hunting with a usurped bow, slaughtering with a usurped knife, praying in usurped land, and selling at the time of the call to prayer – the ruling applies to these actions despite their containing prohibition. By consideration of this principle we said regarding the verse of Allah Almighty: “And never accept their testimony” [al-Nur: 4] that the sinful person is among the people of testimony, so marriage is contracted by the testimony of sinful persons.
