Fard and Wajib in Hanafi Usul: Definitions and Distinctions
Usul definition: linguistic and technical meanings of fard and wajib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of fard and wajib, explain the basis of their distinction (definitive vs. speculative proof), and apply the distinction to identify whether a ruling requires belief.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, fard is established by a decisive proof (qat'i) requiring both action and belief; wajib by a speculative proof (zanni) requiring only action.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi lays the foundation for understanding one of the most important distinctions in Hanafi legal theory: the difference between fard and wajib. While many schools treat all obligations equally, the Hanafis divide them based on the certainty of the evidence. Knowing this distinction is essential for determining the epistemological weight of a ruling and its consequences for faith and practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
Start with the literal meanings: 'fard' means estimation (تقدير) implying fixed limits; 'wajib' means falling or dropping (سقوط), as obligation falls upon the person.
These linguistic roots inform the technical usage.
2
State the technical definitions
In Shari'ah, fard is that which is established by a definitive proof (قطعي) with no doubt. Wajib is established by a proof that contains some doubt (شبهة).
The presence or absence of doubt is the key differentiator.
3
Examine the ruling (hukm) for each
For fard, action and belief are both obligatory; denial causes disbelief. For wajib, only action is obligatory; denial is sinful but not disbelief.
This is the practical consequence of the epistemological distinction.
4
Understand the concept of mudtarib
The text describes wajib as oscillating (مضطرب) between fard and nafl: it is like fard in terms of action (must be done) and like nafl in terms of belief (not required to be held as definitive).
This helps clarify the intermediate status of wajib.
5
Apply the distinction to a concrete example
Consider the witr prayer: it is proven by ahad hadith (zanni), so it is wajib, not fard. Therefore, missing witr is sinful, but denying its obligation does not make one a disbeliever.
This example illustrates the real-world application of the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: الفرض لغة هو التقدير، ومفروضات الشرع مقدراته بحيث لا يحتمل الزيادة والنقصان. وفي الشرع ما ثبت بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه. وحكمه لزوم العمل به والاعتقاد به. والوجوب هو السقوط. والواجب في الشرع هو ما ثبت بدليل فيه شبهة. وحكمه لزوم العمل به دون الاعتقاد. والواجب مضطرب بين الفرض والنفل، فصار فرضا في حق العمل ونفلا في حق الاعتقاد، فلا يلزمنا الاعتقاد به جزما.
Chapter: The fard linguistically is estimation, and the obligatory matters of the Shari'ah are its determined limits such that they do not admit increase or decrease. And technically, it is that which is established by a definitive proof with no doubt in it. Its ruling is necessity of acting upon it and believing in it. And obligation (wujub) is falling/dropping. And the wajib technically is that which is established by a proof that has some doubt in it. Its ruling is necessity of acting upon it but not believing in it. And the wajib is oscillating between fard and nafl, so it becomes obligatory in terms of action and supererogatory in terms of belief; hence we are not required to believe in it definitively.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: fard and wajib. Linguistically, fard means 'estimation' (تقدير). In Shari'ah, fard is a ruling established by a decisive proof (دليل قطعي) with no doubt, such as the obligation of prayer, zakat, and belief in Allah. Its ruling requires both action and definitive belief; denying it leads to disbelief. Wajib is different: it is proven by a proof that has some doubt (شبهة) — for example, the witr prayer. Its ruling requires action, but belief is not mandatory; one who denies it does not become a disbeliever but is a sinner. The difference between fard and wajib in Hanafi usul is that fard is qat'i (certain) in both proof and meaning, while wajib is zanni (speculative). The passage also mentions that 'wujub' means 'falling' (سقوط), because the obligation falls upon the person. The commentator notes that the distinction between fard and wajib is largely terminological, as Shafi'is equate the two. But Hanafis maintain the distinction as a practical principle, particularly for matters of belief.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): A ruling established by definitive proof, requiring both action and belief.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Linguistically means falling/settling; technically the quality of being obligatory.
الواجب (al-wajib): A ruling established by evidence containing some doubt, requiring action but not definitive belief.
مضطرب (mudtarib): Oscillating between two categories (fard and nafl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on believing in a wajib (obligation established by evidence with some doubt)?
A) It is required just like for fard.
B) It is not required; action is required but belief is not.
C) It is recommended but not necessary.
D) It is prohibited to believe in it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, fard is based on definitive proof requiring belief; wajib is based on speculative proof requiring only action.

The Hanafi Hierarchy: Fard, Wajib, and Sunnah
Defining fard, wajib, and sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish fard, wajib, and sunnah according to Hanafi usul, and state the proof level (dalil qati or zanni) that determines each category.

Core Idea
Fard is from decisive proof; wajib from ambiguous proof; sunnah from commendable practice — binding levels differ in belief and action.

This lesson unpacks three foundational categories of legal obligation in the Hanafi school: fard, wajib, and sunnah. Understanding their precise definitions is essential for correctly weighing the binding force of any ruling and for evaluating scholarly disagreements (ikhtilaf) with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms defined
The passage defines al-fard, al-wajib, and al-sunnah in sequence. Locate each definition and note the key characteristics.
Each term has both a linguistic (lughawi) and technical (shar'i) meaning.
2
Analyze the definition of fard
Fard is defined as 'what is established by a decisive proof without any ambiguity' (ma thabata bi-dalil qat'i la shubhata fihi). The consequence: the denier is a disbeliever (yukaffaru jahiduhu). Record the linguistic root (taqdir) and the technical effect.
The proof must be qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalal.
3
Analyze the definition of wajib
Wajib is defined as 'what is established by a proof containing ambiguity' (ma thabata bi-dalil fihi shubha). Examples include an interpreted verse (aya mu'awwala) or a sound solitary hadith (sahih al-ahad). The denier is not a disbeliever. Note the linguistic roots (wujub = falling, wajbah = agitation).
The ambiguity (shubha) can be in transmission or meaning.
4
Analyze the definition of sunnah
Sunnah is defined as 'the commendable followed path in religion' from the Prophet or the Companions. The ruling: one is required to revive it, but not in the manner of fard or wajib. Note the supporting hadith and its evidentiary weight.
The hadith 'Alaykum bi-sunnati...' is a zanni dalil, so sunnah remains below fard/wajib.
5
Compare the three categories
Create a mental table: Fard = qat'i proof, denier kafir, binding in belief and action. Wajib = zanni proof, denier not kafir, binding in action but not belief. Sunnah = commendable but not obligatory, reviving it is encouraged. This is the distinctive Hanafi tripartite hierarchy.
Shafi'is merge fard and wajib; the practical difference appears in cases like witr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل معنى الفرض والسنة الفرض لغة هو التقدير ومفروضات الشرع مقدراته بحيث لا يحتمل الزيادة والنقصان وفي الشرع ما ثبت بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه، حتى يكفر جاحده. وحكمه لزوم العمل به والاعتقاد به، والوجوب هو السقوط يعني: ما يسقط على العبد بلا اختيار منه، وقيل هو من الوجبة وهو الاضطراب أي العبد مضطرب بين الفرض والنفل فصار فرضا في حق العمل أي مترددا ونفلا في حق الاعتقاد فلا يلزمنا الاعتقاد به جزما، وفي الشرع هو ما ثبت بدليل فيه شبهة كالآية المؤوّلة والصحيح من الأحاد، وحكمه ما ذكرنا، والسنّة عبارة عن الطريقه المسلوكة المرضية في باب الدين سواء كانت من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أو من الصحابة، قال عليه السلام عليكم بسنتي وسنة الخلفاء من بعدي عضوا عليها بالنواجذ، وحكمها أن يطالب المرء بإحيائها لا على وجه الفرض والواجب.
Section on the meaning of al-fard and al-sunnah. Al-fard linguistically is estimation, and the obligatory things of the Sharia are its estimations such that they do not admit increase or decrease. In the Sharia, it is that which is established by a decisive proof having no ambiguity, such that its denier becomes a disbeliever. Its ruling is the necessity of acting upon it and believing in it. Al-wujub (obligation) means falling/settling: that which falls upon the servant without his choice. It is also said it is from al-wajbah, which is agitation, i.e., the servant is agitated between fard and nafl, so it becomes fard in terms of action (i.e., wavering) and nafl in terms of belief, so we are not required to believe in it definitively. In the Sharia, it is that which is established by a proof containing ambiguity, such as the interpreted verse and the sound solitary hadith. Its ruling is what we mentioned. Al-sunnah is the commendable followed path in the matter of religion, whether from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) or from the Companions. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Adhere to my sunnah and the sunnah of the rightly-guided caliphs after me; bite onto it with your molars.' Its ruling is that a person is required to revive it, not in the manner of fard or wajib.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This is a usul al-fiqh passage defining the technical terms 'fard', 'wajib', and 'sunnah' in the Hanafi school. The author begins with the linguistic and technical meaning of 'fard'. Linguistically, it means 'estimation' (taqdir); the obligatory matters of the Sharia are its 'estimations' meaning they are fixed limits that cannot be changed. Technically, fard is that which is established by a decisive proof (dalil qati) with no ambiguity whatsoever. The key effect: the denier of a fard becomes a disbeliever (kafir). The ruling includes both acting upon it and believing in it. Then the author defines 'wajib'. He explains the linguistic root: 'wujub' means 'falling' or 'settling' – the obligation falls upon the servant. Another etymology: from 'wajbah' meaning agitation/dizziness, because the servant is agitated between fard and nafl. Thus, the Hanafi school distinguishes wajib from fard: wajib is established by a proof that contains some ambiguity (shubha), such as an interpreted verse (aya mu'awwala) or a sound solitary hadith (sahih al-ahad). It is like fard in action (obligatory to do, leaving it is sinful) but like nafl in belief (the obligation is not definitively certain, so its denier is not called a disbeliever). The passage then defines 'sunnah' linguistically as a path, and technically as the commendable path in religion, sourced from the Prophet or the Companions. The Prophet's hadith (narrated in Ibn Majah and others) commands adherence to his sunnah and that of the caliphs. The ruling of sunnah is that one is encouraged to revive and practice it, but not in the same binding way as fard or wajib. This passage clarifies the Hanafi hierarchy of obligations.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): ما ثبت بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه، ويُكفّر جاحده
الواجب (al-wajib): ما ثبت بدليل ظني فيه شبهة، كالآية المؤوّلة أو خبر الواحد الصحيح، ولا يُكفّر جاحده
السنة (al-sunnah): الطريقة المسلوكة المرضية في الدين من النبي ﷺ أو الصحابة، مطلوب إحياؤها لا على وجه الفرض والوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the sunnah?
A) It is obligatory to believe in it and act upon it.
B) A person is required to revive it, not in the manner of fard or wajib.
C) It is optional; one may do it or leave it without any merit or sin.
D) It is fard because it comes from the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the categories of fard, wajib, and sunnah represent a spectrum of obligation based on the certainty of evidence, with direct implications for takfir and the nature of commitment.

Understanding Sunnah and Nifl in Hanafi Usul
Definitional section on sunnah and nifl from Usul al-Shashi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of sunnah and nifl, and explain the legal consequences (reward, blame, etc.) for each.

Core Idea
Sunnah is rewarded but its abandoner is blameworthy; nifl is purely voluntary with reward but no blame.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines two key categories of non-obligatory acts: sunnah and nifl. While both bring reward, the crucial difference lies in whether abandonment incurs blame. The passage also explains the effect of excuses and the consequences of persistent abandonment. This foundational distinction governs how Hanafi scholars classify a vast range of recommended acts.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of sunnah
Identify what the author says about sunnah: its linguistic meaning and technical definition. Note the blame for abandonment.
The passage states: 'yastaḥiqqu al-lā’imah bi-tarkihā' (deserves blame for leaving it).
2
Identify the exception for excuses
The passage states that blame is removed if the sunnah is left due to a valid excuse (e.g., illness, travel). Understand that this ruling applies to emphasized sunnah.
The commentary clarifies that the ruling applies to sunnah mu’akkada.
3
Define nifl and tatawwu'
The passage defines nifl as increase (ziyāda). Note its synonymy with tatawwu'. Its ruling is reward for performance and no punishment for omission.
Nifl is anything beyond the obligations and duties.
4
Distinguish sunnah mu'akkada from non-emphasized
The commentary explains that the blame applies only to emphasized sunnah; additional sunnahs (sunan zawā'id) follow the ruling of nifl.
Thus, sunan zawā'id are like nawāfil in ruling.
5
Connect to the broader Hanafi hierarchy
Recognize that these definitions complement the concepts of fard and wājib, which are not detailed here but are referenced in the footnote.
The footnote mentions that denial of a qat‘ī sunnah may lead to disbelief, indicating the importance of certain sunnahs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل معنى الفرض والسنة في اللغة. ويستحق اللائمة بتركها إلا أن يتركها بعذر ، والنفل عبارة عن الزيادة، والغنيمة تسمى نفلاً؛ لأنها زيادة على ما هو المقصود من الجهاد، وفي الشرع عبارة عمّا هو زيادة على الفرائض والواجبات، وحكمه أن يثاب المرء على فعله ولا يعاقب بتركه، والنفل والتطوع نظيران. (1) قوله بعذر: كالمرض والنسيان والنوم وكذا السفر على ما قيل إنها تبقى به مندوبة غير مؤكّدة، ثُمّ هذا الحكم في المؤكدة، ومن حكمها أيضاً حرمان الشفاعة عند الإصرار على تركها والتمرن عليه على سنن ما ورد في الحديث، وأما السنن الزوائد فحكمها حكم النوافل والمستحبّات، والمراد بالمطلق هي الهدى أي: المؤكّدة أي: الثابتة بالمواظبة النبوية حقيقةً أو حكماً على وجه العبادة والتشرع، ولا يضره الترك أحياناً، كذا في "الحصول". (2) قوله: [ولا يعاقب ... إلخ] إذا لم يكن الترك مقروناً بالإنكار، وإلا فقد يكفر لو كان قطعي الثبوت، ويفسق في القريب منه، ويكون عاصياً في الظني، و لم يذكر المباح لعدم شموله في سلك الأحكام المكلّف بإتيانها العبد، وحكمه أنه لا يثاب ولا يعاقب بفعله ولا بتركه، وقد يكفر بجحده أيضاً إذا قطع بثبوته، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَاصْطَادُوا﴾ [المائدة:2] أو لم يذكر المكروه تنزيهاً وتحريماً، والحرام؛ لأنها تعريف بمقايسة الفرض والواجب والسنة والنفل كذا في "الحصول". (3) قوله: [نظيران] أي: مثلان في كون كلّ منهما زائداً شرعاً لنا لا علينا إلا أنه كما أن النفل اسم للزيادة، فكذا التطوّع اسم لإتيان خير يؤتى به عن طوع، كذا في "المعدن".
Section on the meaning of fard and sunnah in language. He deserves blame for leaving it unless he leaves it with an excuse. Nifl is an expression for increase. Spoils of war are called nifl because they are an increase over what is intended from jihad. In sacred law, it is an expression for what is an increase over the obligations and duties. Its ruling is that a person is rewarded for doing it and not punished for leaving it. Nifl and tatawwu' are two similar things. (1) His statement: 'with an excuse' — like illness, forgetfulness, sleep, and likewise travel according to what is said that they remain with it as non-emphasized recommend acts. Then this ruling applies to the emphasized ones. And among its rulings is also deprivation of intercession when persisting in leaving it and frequent practice according to what is reported in the hadith. As for the additional sunnahs, their ruling is the same as the supererogatory and recommended acts. What is meant by 'absolute' is the guidance, i.e., the emphasized, i.e., established by prophetic practice actually or virtually in the manner of worship and devotion, and leaving it occasionally does not harm. (2) His statement: 'and not punished, etc.' — if the leaving is not accompanied by denial, otherwise he may become a disbeliever if it is definitively proven, and he becomes a transgressor in what is close to that, and becomes a sinner in what is presumptively proven. The permissible is not mentioned because it is not included in the category of rulings the servant is obligated to perform. Its ruling is that he is neither rewarded nor punished for doing it or leaving it, and he may also disbelieve by denying it if its proof is definitive. (3) His statement: 'two similar things' — i.e., two equals in that each of them is a (voluntary) increase in sacred law for us, not upon us. But just as nifl is a name for increase, so tatawwu' is a name for doing good that is done voluntarily.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section covering the key terms fard, sunnah, and nifl. The author begins by explaining that 'sunnah' in its linguistic sense is a way or path. In fiqh, sunnah is an action whose doer is rewarded and whose abandoner is blamed, except if abandoned due to a valid excuse such as illness, travel, or forgetfulness. The footnote clarifies that the blameworthy abandonment applies specifically to the emphasized sunnah (sunnah mu'akkada), and that persisting in leaving it may deprive one of intercession. Non-emphasized sunnahs (sunan zawā’id) follow the same ruling as supererogatory acts (nawāfil). The author then defines 'nifl' as an increase (ziyāda). In the literal sense, it refers to spoils of war, which are an increase beyond the primary goal of jihad. In sacred law, nifl is anything additional beyond the obligations (farā’iḍ) and duties (wājibāt). Its ruling is pure reward for performance and no punishment for omission. Nifl and tatawwu' are synonyms. The footnote further explains that if abandonment is coupled with denial of the act's status, it may lead to disbelief if the act is definitively proven (qat‘ī al-thubūt). The author does not mention the permissible (mubāḥ) or the disliked (makrūh) and forbidden (ḥarām) because this section focuses on defining the categories that involve reward and blame. This is a standard Hanafi presentation of the hierarchy of rulings.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): An obligation that is established by a definitive proof (dalīl qat‘ī), such as the five daily prayers.
سنة (sunnah): An action that is rewarded but its abandoner is blameworthy, though not punished, unless abandoned with an excuse.
نفل (nifl): A supererogatory act beyond obligations and duties; performer is rewarded, non-performer is not punished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of persistently abandoning an emphasized sunnah without excuse?
A) Punishment in Hell
B) Deprivation of intercession
C) The act becomes wajib
D) No consequence

Key Takeaway
Sunnah mu'akkada is a middle category: rewarded but blameworthy if abandoned without excuse; nifl is purely voluntary with reward and no blame.

Azima and Rukhsa: Definition and Distinction
Defines azima and rukhsa with intra-Hanafi dispute over sunnah/nifl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'azīmah and rukhṣah technically, identify the categories of 'azīmah, and evaluate why sunnah and nifl are excluded from 'azīmah according to the majority Hanafi view.

Core Idea
Azima is the binding original ruling; rukhsa is a concession easing the original command due to an excuse.

This lesson introduces two foundational concepts in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: 'azīmah (resolution) and rukhṣah (concession). Understanding these terms is essential for distinguishing between what is originally required of a Muslim and what is permitted under specific excuses. The passage also addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: whether voluntary acts like sunnah and nifl fall under 'azīmah.

Guided Path
1
Linguistic meaning of 'azīmah
Identify the root meaning of 'azīmah as firm intention and how it relates to legal consequences, e.g., in ẓihār.
The author uses the example of resolution to intercourse being treated as actual return.
2
Technical definition of 'azīmah
Understand 'azīmah in sacred law as 'what is binding on us initially' (mā lazimanā min al-aḥkām ibtidā’an). Note the cause of its firmness.
The firmness stems from the authority of the Commander (God).
3
Categories of 'azīmah
Identify that 'azīmah includes only fard and wājib according to the author. Address the objection regarding haram and makruh.
Haram and makruh are subsumed under fard/wājib and sunnah/mandūb respectively.
4
Intra-Hanafi dispute: sunnah and nifl
Analyze the disagreement: some scholars (e.g., Fakhr al-Islam) include sunnah/nifl in 'azīmah, while the majority exclude them based on the definition.
The majority view relies on 'azīmah being initially binding; sunnah/nifl are not initially binding.
5
Definition of rukhṣah
Understand rukhṣah linguistically (ease) and technically (turning difficulty to ease due to an excuse).
Rukhṣah applies only when a valid shar‘ī excuse exists.

Section on resolution and concession. The chapter: Resolution is the intention when it is in extreme firmness. That is why we said: The resolution to have intercourse is a return in the chapter of ẓihār because it is like the actual occurrence, so it becomes permissible to consider it existing when there is evidence. That is why if someone says 'I resolve' he becomes an oath-taker. In sacred law, it is an expression for the rulings that are binding on us initially. It is called resolution because it is in extreme firmness due to the firmness of its cause, which is that the command is one whose obedience is obligatory by virtue of being our God and we are His slaves. The divisions of resolution are an explanation of what we mentioned: fard and wajib. As for concession, it is an expression for ease and facility. In sacred law, it is turning the command from difficulty to ease by means of an excuse in the obligated person. And its types: (1) His statement: 'The resolution to have intercourse is a return, etc.' — means: a person who performs ẓihār, if he resolves to have intercourse, it is as if he returned to a state of permissibility such that expiation becomes obligatory on him. (2) His statement: 'when evidence is established' — and for that reason the effect of a firm decisive resolution is close to the effect of the act, like the resolution to commit adultery: he is sinful for it even if it is a minor (sin) not a major one, as a reality, as reported: 'The eyes commit adultery, etc.' And his statement: 'That is why, etc.' — means that by 'resolution' is intended the very act resolved upon, metaphorically, due to its complete closeness and connection to it, as if he said 'I bring it into existence'. (3) His statement: 'The divisions of resolution, etc.' — if it is said: The divisions of resolution are more than what he mentioned because the forbidden and the disliked are also divisions of resolution, we reply: The forbidden is included either in the obligatory or in the duty, and the disliked is included in the sunnah or in the recommendable. This is because the forbidden, if established by a definitive proof, then avoiding it is obligatory (fard), like drinking wine; if established by a proof with ambiguity, then avoiding it is a duty (wajib), like eating the ḍabb (a type of lizard); and that which is disliked, its opposite is sunnah or recommendable. Thus, mentioning fard, wajib, and sunnah is a mention of the forbidden and the disliked, so understand. (4) His statement: 'from fard and wajib' — if it is said: Sunnah and nifl are also divisions of resolution, as mentioned by Fakhr al-Islam and his followers, so why did the author not mention them? It is said: Nifl is not from among the resolutions according to some of our companions because it was legislated to compensate for a deficiency that occurred in the resolution, which is fard; likewise, sunnah was legislated to complete the obligations and is dependent on them. This is the position. And therefore, he defined resolution as what is binding on us from the beginning, and the sunnahs and supererogatory acts are not among what is binding on us. (5) His statement: 'In sacred law, turning the command...' — the meaning of turning the command from difficulty to ease is that the original
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section on two key usul concepts: ‘azīmah (resolution) and rukhṣah (concession). The author begins with the linguistic meaning of ‘azīmah as a firm intention. He provides an example from fiqh: in the chapter of ẓihār (an oath repudiating one's wife), a firm resolution to have intercourse is treated as actual return (to conjugal relations), making expiation obligatory. This shows the legal strength of strong intention. In sacred law, ‘azīmah refers to the rulings that are originally binding on the mukallaf (obligated person) without any concession. The cause of its firmness is that the Commander (God) is necessarily obeyed by virtue of being our Lord. The author states that the divisions of ‘azīmah are fard and wājib, as previously discussed. The footnote (3) addresses an objection: what about the forbidden (ḥarām) and disliked (makrūh)? It explains that they are included: ḥarām is subsumed under fard (avoiding it is obligatory by definitive proof) or wājib (avoiding it is duty by presumptive proof), and makrūh is subsumed under sunnah (its opposite is recommended). So mentioning fard and wājib covers the negative categories. Footnote (4) addresses another objection: why aren’t sunnah and nifl included in ‘azīmah? The answer is that according to some Hanafi scholars, nifl is not part of ‘azīmah because it is compensatory for shortcomings in the fard; sunnah is supplementary and not originally binding. The definition of ‘azīmah as 'initially binding' excludes these. The passage then defines rukhṣah linguistically as ease and facility, and technically as turning the original difficult ruling to ease due to an excuse in the mukallaf. The footnote (5) begins the explanation of how the original ruling is turned from difficulty to ease. This is a standard Hanafi usul discussion.

Key Terms
عزيمة (‘azīmah): A ruling that is originally binding on the mukallaf without any concession, due to the firmness of its cause.
رخصة (rukhṣah): A concession that eases a difficult original ruling due to an excuse in the mukallaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Performing extra supererogatory prayers (nawāfil)
B) Performing the five daily obligatory prayers (farā’iḍ)
C) Observing the sunnah mu’akkada in prayer
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, ‘azīmah includes only fard and wājib; sunnah and nifl are not part of it, as they are not originally binding but rather complementary or compensatory.

ʿAzīmah: The Initial Binding Ruling
Definition and scope of ʿazīmah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the technical definition of ʿazīmah, identify its divisions (fard and wajib), and explain the Hanafi position on whether sunnah and nafl are considered ʿazīmah.

Core Idea
ʿAzīmah are rulings binding from the start; only fard and wajib, not sunnah or nafl.

This lesson introduces the foundational usul concept of ʿazīmah. Understanding what constitutes the original binding ruling is crucial before we can discuss exceptions (rukhas). The author defines it linguistically and technically, clarifies its scope, and addresses an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether supererogatory acts fall under ʿazīmah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions
First, locate the passage defining ʿazīmah. Note the linguistic root (emphasis) and how it transforms into a technical term meaning rulings that are binding initially without excuse.
2
Understand the cause of its strength
The text explains that ʿazīmah is strong because its cause (sabab) is that the command of Allah is necessarily obeyed. Recognize that this cause is what makes the ruling emphatic.
3
List the divisions of ʿazīmah
The author explicitly says the divisions are fard and wajib. Commit these two categories as the only components of ʿazīmah in the preferred view.
4
Analyze the dispute about sunnah/nafl
There is a disagreement: some scholars (e.g., Fakhr al-Islam) include sunnah and nafl in ʿazīmah because they compensate for deficiencies in obligations. The author adopts the view that they are not ʿazīmah because they are not initially binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العزيمة والرخصة العزيمة في اللغة: مصدر من التوكيد، وهي القصد إذا كان في نهاية الوكادة، ولهذا قلنا: إن العزيمة عبارة عن قصد مؤكد. وفي الشرع: عبارة عما لزمنا من الأحكام ابتداء؛ سميت عزيمة؛ لأنها في غاية الوكادة لوكادة سببها (وهو كون الأمر مفترض الطاعة بحكم أنه إلهنا ونحن عبيده). وأقسام العزيمة: ما ذكرنا من الفرض والواجب. وأما الرخصة: فعبارة عن اليسر والسهولة. وفي الشرع: صرف الأمر من عسر إلى يسر بواسطة عذر في المكلف. وأنواعها مختلفة لاختلاف أسبابها، وهي أعذار العباد، وفي العاقبة تؤول إلى نوعين: أحدهما: رخصة الفعل مع بقاء الحرمة، بمنزلة العفو في باب الجناية، وذلك نحو إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان مع اطمئنان القلب عند الإكراه، وسب النبي عليه السلام، وإتلاف مال المسلم، وقتل النفس ظلما، وحكمه أنه لو صبر حتى قتل يكون مأجورا لامتناعه عن الحرام تعظيما لنهي الشارع عليه السلام. والنوع الثاني: تغيير صفة الفعل بأن يصير مباحا في حقه، قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ فِي مَحْمَصَة﴾ [المائدة: 3]، وذلك نحو الإكراه على أكل الحرام.
Chapter on 'Azimah and Rukhsah. 'Azimah linguistically: a verbal noun from tawkeed (emphasis), and it is intention when it is at the utmost strength, therefore we say: 'Azimah is an emphasized intention. And technically: it is an expression for what has become binding upon us from rulings initially; it is called 'azimah because it is at the utmost strength due to the strength of its cause (which is that the command is necessarily to be obeyed by virtue that He is our God and we are His servants). And the divisions of 'azimah: what we mentioned of fard and wajib. As for rukhsah: linguistically it is ease and facility. And technically: turning a matter from difficulty to ease by means of an excuse in the responsible person. And its types differ due to the difference of their causes, which are the excuses of the servants, and ultimately they revert to two types: one: a concession of action while the prohibition remains, like the pardon in the chapter of injury, such as uttering a word of disbelief on the tongue while the heart is at ease under duress, and reviling the Prophet (peace be upon him), and destroying the property of a Muslim, and killing a soul unjustly; its ruling is that if he remains patient until he is killed, he is rewarded for refraining from the forbidden out of reverence for the prohibition of the Lawgiver (peace be upon him). And the second type: changing the description of the act so that it becomes permissible for him, Allah says: "But whoever is forced in hunger" [al-Ma'ida: 3], such as coercion to eat what is forbidden.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a definition of two key terms in usul al-fiqh: 'azimah and rukhsah. The author first defines 'azimah linguistically as an emphatic intention, then technically as rulings that are binding initially. The binding nature comes from the fact that Allah, as our Creator and Master, has the authority to command, and His commands are at the highest level of emphasis. The divisions of 'azimah are fard and wajib, which are the two categories of obligatory acts. Optional acts like sunnah and nafl are not included in 'azimah according to the view adopted by the author, though there is a disagreement among Hanafi scholars on this point. Next, rukhsah is defined linguistically as ease, and technically as a legal ruling that shifts from difficulty to ease due to an excuse in the person. There are two types of rukhsah: (1) The act is permitted but the original prohibition remains in place, like the allowance to utter disbelief under duress while the heart is firm in faith. In such cases, the sin is removed from the action, but the prohibition is not lifted entirely; the person is rewarded if they choose to bear the hardship instead. (2) The act becomes genuinely permissible, changing its legal description, such as eating carrion when starving to death. The Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Ma'idah is evidence for this second type.

Key Terms
العزيمة (al-‘azimah): اللازم ابتداء من الأحكام الشرعية بقوة ووكادة دون عذر (what is initially binding from legal rulings with strength and emphasis without excuse)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the divisions of ʿazīmah?
A) Fard and Wajib
B) Fard, Wajib, and Sunnah
C) Fard, Wajib, Sunnah, and Nafl
D) Only Fard

Key Takeaway
ʿAzīmah refers to original binding rulings (fard and wajib) without excuse; sunnah and nafl are not included.

Rukhsah: Concession Due to Excuse
Definition and types of rukhsah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define rukhsah technically, distinguish its two types (concession with remaining prohibition vs. change of description), and apply them to concrete examples.

Core Idea
Rukhsah turns difficulty to ease due to an excuse; it may keep the prohibition or change the act's description to permissible.

Rukhsah is the counterpart to ʿazīmah. While ʿazīmah represents the original ruling, rukhsah provides relief when an excuse is present. This lesson examines its linguistic and technical definitions, then unpacks the two main types with examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Define rukhsah linguistically and technically
Locate the definition: linguistic meaning (ease), technical meaning (turning from difficulty to ease due to an excuse). Note the key element: ' بواسطة عذر في المكلف '.
2
Identify the two types of rukhsah
The author divides rukhsah into two ultimate categories: (1) concession of action while the original prohibition remains, and (2) changing the description of the act to permissible.
3
Examine examples for the first type
The text gives examples: uttering disbelief under duress while heart is firm, reviling the Prophet (out of duress), destroying property, killing unjustly. Understand that the act itself remains haram, but the doer is excused and may even be rewarded for refraining if possible.
4
Examine examples for the second type
The second type changes the act to mubah (permissible), as in eating carrion when starving. The Quranic verse is cited as evidence. The act becomes halal for the person due to necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العزيمة والرخصة العزيمة في اللغة: مصدر من التوكيد، وهي القصد إذا كان في نهاية الوكادة، ولهذا قلنا: إن العزيمة عبارة عن قصد مؤكد. وفي الشرع: عبارة عما لزمنا من الأحكام ابتداء؛ سميت عزيمة؛ لأنها في غاية الوكادة لوكادة سببها (وهو كون الأمر مفترض الطاعة بحكم أنه إلهنا ونحن عبيده). وأقسام العزيمة: ما ذكرنا من الفرض والواجب. وأما الرخصة: فعبارة عن اليسر والسهولة. وفي الشرع: صرف الأمر من عسر إلى يسر بواسطة عذر في المكلف. وأنواعها مختلفة لاختلاف أسبابها، وهي أعذار العباد، وفي العاقبة تؤول إلى نوعين: أحدهما: رخصة الفعل مع بقاء الحرمة، بمنزلة العفو في باب الجناية، وذلك نحو إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان مع اطمئنان القلب عند الإكراه، وسب النبي عليه السلام، وإتلاف مال المسلم، وقتل النفس ظلما، وحكمه أنه لو صبر حتى قتل يكون مأجورا لامتناعه عن الحرام تعظيما لنهي الشارع عليه السلام. والنوع الثاني: تغيير صفة الفعل بأن يصير مباحا في حقه، قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ فِي مَحْمَصَة﴾ [المائدة: 3]، وذلك نحو الإكراه على أكل الحرام.
Chapter on 'Azimah and Rukhsah. 'Azimah linguistically: a verbal noun from tawkeed (emphasis), and it is intention when it is at the utmost strength, therefore we say: 'Azimah is an emphasized intention. And technically: it is an expression for what has become binding upon us from rulings initially; it is called 'azimah because it is at the utmost strength due to the strength of its cause (which is that the command is necessarily to be obeyed by virtue that He is our God and we are His servants). And the divisions of 'azimah: what we mentioned of fard and wajib. As for rukhsah: linguistically it is ease and facility. And technically: turning a matter from difficulty to ease by means of an excuse in the responsible person. And its types differ due to the difference of their causes, which are the excuses of the servants, and ultimately they revert to two types: one: a concession of action while the prohibition remains, like the pardon in the chapter of injury, such as uttering a word of disbelief on the tongue while the heart is at ease under duress, and reviling the Prophet (peace be upon him), and destroying the property of a Muslim, and killing a soul unjustly; its ruling is that if he remains patient until he is killed, he is rewarded for refraining from the forbidden out of reverence for the prohibition of the Lawgiver (peace be upon him). And the second type: changing the description of the act so that it becomes permissible for him, Allah says: "But whoever is forced in hunger" [al-Ma'ida: 3], such as coercion to eat what is forbidden.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a definition of two key terms in usul al-fiqh: 'azimah and rukhsah. The author first defines 'azimah linguistically as an emphatic intention, then technically as rulings that are binding initially. The binding nature comes from the fact that Allah, as our Creator and Master, has the authority to command, and His commands are at the highest level of emphasis. The divisions of 'azimah are fard and wajib, which are the two categories of obligatory acts. Optional acts like sunnah and nafl are not included in 'azimah according to the view adopted by the author, though there is a disagreement among Hanafi scholars on this point. Next, rukhsah is defined linguistically as ease, and technically as a legal ruling that shifts from difficulty to ease due to an excuse in the person. There are two types of rukhsah: (1) The act is permitted but the original prohibition remains in place, like the allowance to utter disbelief under duress while the heart is firm in faith. In such cases, the sin is removed from the action, but the prohibition is not lifted entirely; the person is rewarded if they choose to bear the hardship instead. (2) The act becomes genuinely permissible, changing its legal description, such as eating carrion when starving to death. The Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Ma'idah is evidence for this second type.

Key Terms
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): صرف الأمر من عسر إلى يسر بواسطة عذر في المكلف (turning a ruling from difficulty to ease by means of an excuse in the responsible person)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second type of rukhsah according to the passage?
A) A concession with the prohibition remaining
B) A complete cancellation of the ruling
C) A change in the description of the act so it becomes permissible
D) A replacement of the act with a different act

Key Takeaway
Rukhsah provides relief due to an excuse; it either leaves the prohibition intact while excusing the action or changes the act to permissible.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Two Types of Rukhsa in Hanafi Usul
Defines and distinguishes the two categories of rukhsa in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of rukhsa in Hanafi usul by identifying the legal treatment of the act under each type and the correct Quranic evidence for the second type.

Core Idea
Rukhsa either treats a prohibited act as permissible while prohibition remains, or actually lifts the prohibition making it truly permissible.

This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul al-Fiqh defines the key distinction in Hanafi thought between the two categories of rukhsa (concession). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings in cases of duress (ikrah) and necessity (darura). The text also clarifies the appropriate Quranic proof for actual permissibility (Surah al-An'am 6:119) as opposed to mere removal of sin.

Guided Path
1
Define 'Azima and Rukhsa
Note the definition of azima as the original ruling that is permanently required, and rukhsa as a concession due to an excuse. Understand that the five daily prayers are not rukhsa because the original heavy obligation was never actually legislated.
Azima is the baseline; rukhsa is the exception.
2
Identify the Two Types
First type: the original prohibition remains, but the act is treated as permissible for the excuse (e.g., uttering disbelief under coercion when heart is at peace). Second type: the prohibition is lifted and the act becomes truly permissible (e.g., eating carrion under duress).
The distinction hinges on whether the ruling changes in essence or only in treatment.
3
Analyze the Legal Consequence
For the first type, if one endures death rather than committing the act, he is rewarded. For the second type, if one refrains from the permissible act and dies, he is sinful (like one who kills himself) if he knew the ruling.
The reward/sin outcome distinguishes the two types practically.
4
Examine the Quranic Proof
The author argues that Surah al-An'am 6:119 ("except that to which you are compelled") is stronger evidence for actual permissibility than Surah al-Ma'idah 5:3, because the exception removes the prohibition entirely, not merely the sin.
This is a usul point: an exception from a general prohibition (istithna' min amm) indicates permissibility.
5
Apply the Distinction
Recognize practical cases: insulting the Prophet under duress (first type) vs. consuming forbidden food (second type). The ruling for each depends on which type applies.
Proper classification is essential for correct fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العزيمة والرخصة
العزيمة ما لزم العبد من أحكام الشرع على وجه العزيمة، ثم تسقط شرعيته بواسطة عذر في المكلف، فلا يرد أن الصلوات الخمس من العزيمة بالاتفاق مع أن فيها صرف الأمر من عسر إلى يسر، فإن النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم أمر ليلة المعراج بخمسين صلاة، وإنما لا يرد؛ لأن الأصل وهو ما زاد على الخمس لم يكن مشروعا فلم يكن رخصة إلا مجازا لما فيه من التخفيف واليسر.
أما الرخصة فلها أنواع مختلفة لاختلاف أسبابها وهي أعذار العباد، وفي العاقبة تؤول إلى نوعين:
أحدهما رخصة الفعل مع بقاء الحرمة بمنزلة العفو في باب الجناية وذلك نحو إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان مع اطمئنان القلب عند الإكراه وسب النبي عليه السلام وإتلاف مال المسلم وقتل النفس ظلما، وحكمه أنه لو صبر حتى قتل يكون مأجورا لامتناعه عن الحرام تعظيما لنهي الشارع عليه السلام.
والنوع الثاني: تغيير صفة الفعل بأن يصير مباحا في حقه، قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ فِي مَحْمَصَة﴾ [المائدة: 3] وذلك نحو الإكراه على أكل الميتة وشرب الخمر، وحكمه أنه لو امتنع عن تناوله حتى قتل يكون آثما بامتناعه عن المباح وصار كقاتل نفسه.
وذكر في الحواشي: قوله [مع بقاء الحرمة] أي: يعامل معه معاملة المباح لعذر وحرج لا أنه يغير حكمه ويعتبر مباحا في حقيقة، وتظهر ثمرة الفرق بين المباح الحقيقي والحكمي فيما سيأتي من الأمر والإثم في ترك اختيار الرخصة، والمراد بمنزلة معاملته معاملة المباح هو عدم ترتب حكمه من العقاب على فعله، كما مثل المصنف بالعفو عن موجب الجناية، فإنه لا يكون به الجناية مباحة غير حرام. وقوله [نحو إجراء كلمة الكفر ... إلخ] فإن حرمة الكفر قائمة لوجوب حق الله تعالى في الإيمان، لكن رخص لعذر وهو أن حق العبد في نفسه يفوت القتل صورة بتخريب البينة ومعنى بزهاق الروح، وحق الله تعالى لا يفوت معنى؛ لأن التصديق قائم، وإنما يفوت صورة؛ لأن الأصل هو التصديق، وذلك باق فرخص له الإقدام رعاية للحقين أو ترجيحا لحقه. وقوله [وقتل النفس ظلما] فإن حرمة قتله باقية؛ لأنه معصوم الدم بإيمانه، لكنه عذر في قتله للضرورة فلا يؤخذ بالقصاص، لكنه لم يسعه أن يقدم على قتله، بل يصبر حتى يقتل، فإن قتله كان آثما؛ لأن قتل المسلم لا يباح بوجه ما إلا لمعان ثلاث. وقوله [فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ فِي مَحْمَصَة الآية] فإنها لا تفيد أصل الإباحة بل رفع الإثم، ومفاده أن يعامل معه معاملة المباح فلا يعطي أصل المقصود، فالمناسب أن يستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَقَدْ فَصَّلَ لَكُم مَّا حَرَّمَ عَلَيْكُمْ إِلَّا مَا اضْطُرِرْتُمْ﴾ [الأنعام: 119]، فإنه استثناء من مفعول حَرَّمَ، فلا يكون المضطر إليه محرما فيكون مباحا. وقوله [يكون آثما] وإنما يأثم إذا علم بالإباحة ولم يأكل حتى قتل وإلا فيعذر بالجهل فيه كالجهل بالخطاب في دار الحرب. وقوله [كقاتل نفسه] هذا لأن حرمته ما ثبت إلا صيانة لعقله ودينه عن فساد الخمر ونفسه عن الميتة لتعدي خبث الميتة إلى بدنه، فإذا خاف بالإكراه فوات نفسه لم يستقم صيانة للبعض أي العقل لفوات الكل فسقط المحرم فكان الحرمة أيضا ساقطة.
Section on Azima and Rukhsa. Azima is what is required of the servant from the rulings of the Sharia in the manner of resolution, then its legality falls due to an excuse in the obligated person. It is not objected that the five prayers are azima by consensus even though there is a change of the command from hardship to ease, for the Prophet commanded fifty prayers on the night of the Mi'raj; this is not an objection because the original (the extra beyond five) was not legislated, so it was not rukhsa except metaphorically due to the easing and facility. As for rukhsa, it has different types due to different causes, which are the excuses of the servants, and in the end they reduce to two types: One is rukhsa of the act with the prohibition remaining, similar to pardon in the chapter of crime, such as uttering the word of disbelief on the tongue while the heart is at peace under coercion, and insulting the Prophet, and destroying the property of a Muslim, and wrongfully killing a soul. Its ruling is that if he perseveres until he is killed, he is rewarded for refraining from the prohibited out of reverence for the prohibition of the Lawgiver. The second type is changing the attribute of the act so that it becomes permissible for him. Allah said: {But whoever is forced in hunger} [al-Ma'idah: 3] such as coercion to eat carrion and drink wine. Its ruling is that if he refrains from consuming it until he is killed, he becomes sinful due to his refraining from what is permissible and becomes like one who kills himself. In the commentary: 'His saying [with the prohibition remaining] means that it is treated as permissible due to an excuse and hardship, not that it alters its ruling and is considered permissible in reality. The fruit of the difference between real and de facto permissibility appears in what comes regarding command and sin in leaving the choice of rukhsa. The meaning of 'like pardon' is the non-application of the ruling of punishment for the act, as the author exemplified with pardon for the cause of crime, for by it the crime does not become permissible and non-prohibited.' 'His saying [such as uttering the word of disbelief...]' The prohibition of disbelief remains due to the obligation of Allah's right in faith, but a license is given due to an excuse: the right of the servant in his life is lost in form by destruction and in meaning by loss of spirit, while Allah's right is not lost in meaning because belief remains, only lost in form; so he is licensed to proceed out of consideration for both rights or giving preference to his right. 'His saying [and wrongfully killing a soul]' The prohibition of killing him remains because he is inviolable due to his faith, but there is an excuse for killing him out of necessity, so he is not subject to retaliation; however, it is not permissible for him to proceed to kill him, but he should persevere until he is killed; if he kills, he is sinful because killing a Muslim is not permitted in any way except for three reasons. 'His saying [But whoever is forced in hunger...]' This verse does not indicate original permissibility but only removal of sin, implying it is treated as permissible, so it does not give the original objective; rather, it is appropriate to cite His saying: {And He has detailed for you what He has forbidden to you except that to which you are compelled} [al-An'am: 119], for it is an exception from the object of 'forbidden', so the compelled thing is not forbidden and thus permissible. 'His saying [he becomes sinful]' He only becomes sinful if he knows of the permissibility and does not eat until killed; otherwise he is excused due to ignorance, like ignorance of the address in the land of war. 'His saying [like one who kills himself]' This is because its prohibition was only established to protect his intellect and religion from the corruption of wine and his body from carrion due to the transmission of its foulness; if under coercion he fears for his life, it is not appropriate to protect a part (the intellect) at the loss of the whole, so the prohibition falls and the ruling (of prohibition) also falls.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul defines the concepts of azima and rukhsa (resolution and license) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first clarifies that azima is a ruling that is permanently required, and any perceived easing (like reducing prayers from fifty to five) is not a true rukhsa because the original heavy obligation was never actually legislated. The main focus is on rukhsa, which is divided into two types based on whether the original prohibition remains or is removed. The first type, rukhsa with the prohibition remaining, is illustrated by acts like uttering disbelief under coercion where the inner faith remains intact. The act is treated as permissible for the sake of preserving life, but the prohibition is not actually lifted; hence if one endures killing, he is rewarded. The second type, rukhsa that changes the act to permissible, is illustrated by eating carrion or drinking wine under duress. Here the prohibition is removed, and the act becomes truly permissible. The author then provides a detailed commentary explaining the rationale and nuances, including the appropriate Quranic evidence (citing al-An'am: 119 as the strong proof for actual permissibility) and the conditions for sin in refraining. This lesson is fundamental for understanding how Hanafi usul balances textual commands with practical concessions.

Key Terms
العزيمة (al-ʿazīmah): A ruling that is permanently required from the servant without any excuse causing it to fall; it is the original ruling of the Sharia.
الرخصة (al-rukhṣah): A concession that allows an otherwise prohibited act due to an excuse, either with the prohibition remaining (only treatment as permissible) or with the prohibition lifted (actual permissibility).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, what is the difference between the two types of rukhsa?
A) The first type applies only to acts of worship, the second to worldly acts.
B) In the first type, the prohibition remains but the act is treated as permissible; in the second, the prohibition is lifted and the act becomes truly permissible.
C) The first type is for cases of coercion, the second for cases of ignorance.
D) The first type is revocable, the second is permanent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, rukhsa either maintains the original prohibition (only treating the act as permissible for the excuse) or actually lifts the prohibition, making the act truly mubah.
